The Sixth Proof: Distinction Through Accidents
Al-Jurjani presents the sixth proof for the origination of bodies based on distinction through accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth proof for the origination of bodies, which argues that bodies cannot exist without being distinguished from one another, and this distinction is only possible through accidents that are themselves originated.

Core Idea
Bodies need accidents to be distinct, and accidents are originated, so bodies are originated.

Al-Jurjani now presents the sixth proof, building on earlier demonstrations that accidents are originated. The core insight is that bodies, composed of similar individual atoms, require accidents to be distinguished from each other. Since accidents are proven originated, the bodies themselves must also be originated. This proof is distinct from the earlier ones because it focuses on the necessity of distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the initial claim that bodies cannot exist without differentiation, and how the author connects this to the nature of atoms and accidents.
The passage is dense and includes repetition from earlier proofs; focus on the opening argument.
2
Identify the key premises
The proof rests on two premises: (1) All bodies must be distinct from each other. (2) Distinction is only possible through accidents. Since accidents are already proven originated, bodies are also originated.
3
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
The common misunderstanding is confusing this proof with the first proof. The first proof argues that bodies cannot exist without accidents; this proof argues that bodies cannot be distinct without accidents.
The passage also repeats the second aspect about motion and rest, but that is a recap of earlier material.

The second observatory – the first goal: originated bodies are four divisions. The sixth of this observatory: And since bodies do not exist without differentiation between them, because every existent must necessarily be distinguished from another existent, and we have shown that differentiation between bodies is only through accidents, based on the similarity of the individual atoms from which bodies are composed; then accidents are originated because they do not endure for two moments, and everything that is like that is originated; and their proof has already passed—i.e., the proof that differentiation between bodies is only through accidents, and the proof that accidents do not endure for two moments—and if he had confined himself to mentioning the proof of the second it would have been better because he said: 'And we have shown.' The second of the two aspects: it is said: the body is not free from motion and rest, and they are two originated things; so the body is not free from accidents. We only say that the body is not free from them because it is not free from being in a place by necessity. If its being in that place is preceded by a being (i.e., another being) in that place, then it is at rest, because rest is the second being in the first place; otherwise (i.e., if its being in that place is not preceded by a being in it) then it is moving. It cannot be said: your proof is refuted by the body in the first moment of its occurrence, because it applies to it while it is neither moving nor at rest, since it is not then qualified by a second being, neither in the first place nor in the second place. For we say: the discussion is about the remaining body; so it is claimed that it is not free from motion or rest, not about the newly originated body, so there is no refutation. And if this question is posed as a contradiction, it is a rejection that does not harm the one giving the reason, because his purpose is the origination of the body. We only say that motion is originated for several reasons: First: The essence of motion is preceded by the other, i.e., its essence necessitates precedence by its very nature because it is from one state to another; rather we say: it is the second being in another place, so it is preceded by the first state and the first being. And the essence of eternity is non-precedence by the other, and between them is an essential opposition, so motion cannot be eternal, and that is the meaning of the originated. Second: The essence does not exist except in particular instances because the absolute cannot be conceived as existing separately from all determinations; and there is no doubt that none of the particular instances of motion exist in eternity, because each part of it is divisible into parts that cannot be gathered together, so they only exist successively; so its essence also does not exist in it (i.e., in eternity), so its essence is originated like its particulars. Third: Every particular motion is preceded by an eternal non-existence, so the non-existences (i.e., the non-existences of all particular motions) are gathered in
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is the presentation of the sixth proof for the origination of bodies, though it also repeats some of the second aspect from the first proof. The proof begins with the claim that bodies do not exist without being distinct from one another, and this distinctness is necessarily through accidents, because the individual atoms composing bodies are similar. Accidents themselves are originated because they do not endure for two moments. The author then refers to the previous explanation of these two points: that distinctness is only through accidents, and that accidents do not endure. He notes that it would have been sufficient to mention only the second proof (about accidents not enduring). Then the text repeats the second aspect from the first proof: the body is never free from motion or rest, both of which are originated. This is justified by the body necessarily occupying a place; if it is preceded in the same place, it is rest; otherwise motion. The objection about the first moment is answered by restricting the argument to persistent bodies, not newly originated ones. The passage then lists three reasons for motion being originated: (1) The essence of motion is defined as being preceded by another state, whereas eternity is non-precedence; they are incompatible. (2) The essence of motion exists only in particular instances, and since particular motions cannot exist all at once in eternity, the essence itself is originated. (3) Every particular motion is preceded by an eternal non-existence, so the non-existences are all gathered together (the text cuts off here).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sixth proof is essentially the same as the first proof.
Correction: The first proof argues that bodies cannot exist without accidents; the sixth proof argues that bodies cannot be distinct without accidents. They are distinct arguments, though both lead to the origination of bodies.

?

The Correlation Proof for Finite Motions
Al-Iji presents the fifth proof using the method of correlation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the method of correlation (tadhāyuf) demonstrates that an infinite series of motions is impossible because the correlatives 'preceding' and 'preceded' must be equal in number.

Core Idea
Preceding and preceded must be equal, so an infinite series is impossible.

Al-Iji now presents a proof based on the logical relationship between correlatives. He takes a series of motions (days) and argues that if it were infinite, the number of 'preceded' parts would exceed the number of 'preceding' parts by one. But because 'preceding' and 'preceded' are true correlatives, they must be equal in number. This contradiction proves the series must be finite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own flow come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Iji uses a compressed style and assumes familiarity with the method of correlation.
2
Identify the key correlatives
Identify 'preceding' (sābiq) and 'preceded' (masbūq) as the two correlatives. Understand that they are 'real correlatives' (mutadhāyifān ḥaqīqiyān) meaning they come in pairs and must exist in equal number.
The term 'tadhāyuf' refers to the mutual relation between two things where each is defined in terms of the other.
3
Understand the counting argument
Follow the setup: take the present day as the 'last' part. In an infinite chain, every part except the last is both preceding and preceded. The last is only preceded. Thus the count of preceded parts exceeds preceding parts by one.
This imbalance is what contradicts the nature of correlatives.
4
Consider the nuance about father-son
Al-Iji notes that equality of 'well-known' terms (like father and son) is not strictly required unless we make a conceptual distinction. However, in the pure correlative relation, equality holds. This shows the proof is rigorous.
This nuance prevents a potential objection.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The contradiction forces us to reject the hypothesis of an infinite series. Therefore, the series of motions is finite.
This proof is one of several ways to establish that motions are created and limited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : من تلك الوجوه طريقة التضايف وقد عرفتها أيضا هناك وتقريرها هنا أن الحركات تتألف من أجزاء بعضها سابقة وبعضها مسبوقة ولنجعلها أياماً مثلاً فلو كانت تلك الأيام غير متناهية أمكن لنا أن نجعل من يوم ما وهو اليوم الذي نحن فيه جزءاً أخيراً فنقول : هذا الجزء في هذه السلسلة التي لا تتناهى مسبوق أي موصوف بالمسبوقية وليس بسابق وكل جزء من أجزائها الأخر سابق ومسبوق بحسب الفرض إذ المفروض لا تناهي السلسلة فكل واحد من أجزائها الأخر موصوف بالمسبوقية والسابقية معاً إذ لو وجد فيها سابق غير موصوف بالمسبوقية لانقطعت السلسلة به وعلى هذا التقدير فكل سابق مسبوق من غير عكس كلي كالأخير المذكور فيكون عدد المسبوق أي المسبوقية أزيد من عدد السابق أي السابقية بواحد وأنه محال لأنهما متضايفان حقيقيان يجب تكافؤهما في الوجود وتساويهما في العدد وأن يكون بإزاء كل واحد من أحدهما واحد من الآخر. وأما تساوي عدد المشهوريين فغير لازم كأب واحد له أبناء إلا أن يعتبر التغاير الاعتباري بحسب الوصف، ولو كانت السلسلة متناهية كان هناك سابق ليس بمسبوق فيتكافأ الإضافيان
The fifth: from those ways is the method of correlation, and you have already known it there. Its formulation here is that motions are composed of parts, some of which are preceding and some preceded. Let us make them, for example, days. So if those days were infinite, it would be possible for us to make from a certain day—which is the day we are in—a final part and we say: this part in this chain which does not terminate is preceded, i.e., described with precededness, and is not preceding, and every other part of its parts is preceding and preceded according to the hypothesis, for the hypothesis is that the chain is not finite, so every one of its other parts is described with both precededness and precedingness together, for if there were found in it a preceding not described with precededness, the chain would be cut off by it. On this estimation, every preceding is preceded without full converse like the mentioned last part, so the number of preceded, i.e., precededness, is greater than the number of preceding, i.e., precedingness, by one, and that is impossible because they are two real correlatives that must be equivalent in existence and equal in number, and that for every one of the one there be one from the other. As for the equality of the number of the two well-known ones, it is not necessary, like one father having sons, unless a conceptual distinction is considered according to description. And if the chain were finite, there would be a preceding not preceded, so the two correlatives would be equivalent.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof for the finitude of motions, known as the method of correlation (tadhāyuf). The author sets up an infinite series of motions, here likened to days. He chooses a specific day (the present) as the last part of the series. This last part is described as 'preceded' (masbūq) but not 'preceding' (sābiq). Every other part in the infinite series is both preceded and preceding. Consequently, the total number of preceded items exceeds the number of preceding items by one (since the last is only preceded). However, 'preceded' and 'preceding' are real correlatives (mutadhāyifān ḥaqīqiyān) that must be numerically equal in existence; for every one of the one category there must be exactly one from the other. An inequality is impossible, so the series cannot be infinite. The author adds a nuance: the equality of the two 'well-known' terms is not strictly required if we consider father-son relationships, but that is due to a conceptual distinction; in a purely correlative sense, equality holds. If the series were finite, there would be a first 'preceding' that is not 'preceded', restoring equality. Thus, the motions must be finite.

Key Terms
تضايف
سابق
مسبوق
متضايفان حقيقيان
تكافؤ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that in an infinite series, the number of preceding and preceded parts could be equal, but the proof shows that taking a specific point as last forces an inequality.
Correction: The proof demonstrates that if you pick any point as 'last' in an infinite series, that point is only preceded, causing a numerical imbalance. Since the correlatives must be equal, the series cannot be infinite.

Checkpoint
According to the method of correlation, why can't the series of motions be infinite?
A) Because every part is both preceding and preceded.
B) Because the number of preceded parts exceeds the number of preceding parts by one, contradicting the necessary equality of correlatives.
C) Because the series would have no beginning.
D) Because motions are contingent.

Key Takeaway
The correlation proof shows that an infinite series of motions is impossible because it would violate the necessary numerical equality of the correlatives 'preceding' and 'preceded'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that your life is part of a finite series of days. If every day you have lived is preceded and will be followed by a limited number of days, how does that reality change the way you will spend the day you are in right now? Will you live it as if it is just one in an infinite chain, or as a precious, non-repeatable moment?
?
The proof shows that correlatives must be equal; an imbalance is impossible. In your relationship with your Creator, are you living in 'balance'—responding to His blessings with gratitude and obedience? Or have you allowed an imbalance to grow, taking His favors without properly acknowledging them as preceded?
?
If the chain of your actions is finite, then every deed you do today is a 'preceded' act that will be followed by consequences. Are you aware that your current actions are not just isolated but part of a finite series leading to an ultimate judgment? What specific step can you take today to ensure that your account of 'preceding' good deeds outweighs the 'preceded' bad ones?

Second Proof: No Eternal Body
Al-Iji presents a second proof against an eternal body using the necessity of a first 'being' in place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof against an eternal body, which argues that an eternal body would require either an eternal 'being' in place or an infinite regress of 'beings', both of which are rationally impossible.

Core Idea
An eternal body would demand an impossible first 'being' or an infinite chain.

Al-Iji presents a second, more concise proof that no body can be eternal. The argument focuses on the body's need for a spatial 'being' (kawn) at every moment. It shows that this leads to either an eternal 'being' within the body or an infinite chain, both of which are rationally impossible. This proof sharpens the earlier argument by clearly framing the two exhaustive possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the logical structure: if an eternal body existed, then two possibilities for its 'being' arise.
The passage is incomplete at the end, but the core argument is clear.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage states: either the body has a 'being' not preceded by another (first division), or every 'being' is preceded by another ad infinitum (second division). Locate these in the text.
3
Understand why both are impossible
The first division implies an eternal 'being' within the body, which is impossible because an eternal 'being' cannot cease (contradicting observed change). The second division implies an infinite regress, also impossible. Reflect on why each is rejected.
The author notes the second division needs no further explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثاني وهو لبعض المتأخرين كالاختصار للمسلك الأول أنه لو وجد جسم قديم لزم إما كون واحد قديم وإما أن يكون قبل كل كون كون آخر لا إلى نهاية والتالي باطل بقسميه أما الملازمة فلأنه لا بد للجسم من كون في حيز لكونه متحيزاً بالذات فإن وجد له كون غير مسبوق بآخر أي بكون آخر لزم القسم الأول لأن ذلك الكون يجب أن يكون ثابتاً للجسم القديم على الاستمرار فيكون قديما وإلا أي وإن لم يوجد له كون غير مسبوق بآخر لزم القسم الثاني لأن كل كون له فإنه مسبوق يكون آخر فوجب أن يكون قبل كل كون كون لا إلى نهاية إذ على ذلك التقدير الذي نحن فيه لو وجد كون لا كون قبله لزم خلو الجسم عن الكون وأنت خبير بأن القسم الثاني لا يحتاج إلى هذا البيان لأنه إذا لم يوجد له كون غير مسبوق بآخر كان كل كون له مسبوقاً بكون قبله لا إلى نهاية إنما المحتاج إلى البيان هو القسم الأول، بأن يقال : ذلك الكون الذي ليس مسبوقاً أن يكون مستمراً أزلاً وإلا لزم خلو الجسم عن الكون، نعم لو قيل : إن بمثله يجب
The second path, and it is by some of the later scholars, like an abridgment of the first path: that if an eternal body existed, there would necessarily be either one eternal 'being' or that before every 'being' there is another 'being' without end, and the consequent is false in both divisions. As for the entailment, it is because the body must have a 'being' in a place, due to its being essentially occupying space. So if it has a 'being' not preceded by another, i.e., by another 'being', the first division follows, because that 'being' must be constant for the eternal body continuously, so it is eternal. And if not, i.e., if it does not have a 'being' not preceded by another, the second division follows, because every 'being' of it is preceded by another 'being', so there must be before every 'being' a 'being' without end, since on that estimation which we are considering, if there were a 'being' with no 'being' before it, the body would be empty of 'being'. And you are aware that the second division does not need this explanation, because if it does not have a 'being' not preceded by another, then every 'being' of it is preceded by a 'being' before it without end. Rather, what needs explanation is the first division, by saying: that 'being' which is not preceded must be continuous eternally, otherwise the body would be empty of 'being'. Yes, if it were said that like it must [exist]...
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a second proof (al-maslak al-thānī) attributed to some later scholars, summarizing the first proof. It argues that if a body were eternal (qadīm), it must occupy a spatial 'being' (kawn) at every moment. Two possibilities arise: either the body has a 'being' that is not preceded by any other 'being', or every 'being' is preceded by another ad infinitum. The first possibility entails an eternal 'being' that continuously belongs to the body, which is impossible because an eternal existential cannot cease (as shown earlier). The second possibility entails an infinite regress of 'beings', which is also impossible. The author notes that the second division (infinite regress) is obviously absurd and needs no further explanation. The first division requires elaboration: the unpreceded 'being' must be eternally continuous; otherwise, the body would have a moment without 'being', which is impossible for a body that essentially occupies space. The text cuts off, but the argument is clearly that both options are impossible, so no body can be eternal. This proof relies on the impossibility of both an eternal 'being' (due to its ceasing being impossible, contradicting observed motion) and infinite regress.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'being' (kawn) refers to the body itself, but it actually means the body's specific spatial location at a given time.
Correction: The 'being' is not the body but its location. The argument concerns the impossibility of an infinite series of locations or an eternal location for a body.

Checkpoint
According to the second path, if a body is eternal, what are the two possibilities for its 'being' in place?
A) It is either necessary or contingent.
B) It is either eternal or caused.
C) It is either not preceded by another being or preceded by an infinite series.
D) It is either simple or compound.

Key Takeaway
The second path proves that an eternal body is impossible because its necessary spatial 'being' would lead to either an eternal 'being' or an infinite regress, both of which are impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever cling to a possession, relationship, or status as if it will never fade, forgetting that nothing besides Allah is eternal? How does the proof that no eternal body exists challenge you to release your grip on created things?
?
The argument shows that every 'being' (every moment and state you experience) is preceded by another, pointing to a First Cause. When you look at your own existence, do you truly feel the weight that you are not self-sufficient but contingent upon Allah at every instant?
?
If an eternal body is impossible, what does that imply about anything you rely on other than Allah? Is there any created thing you treat as if it will never end? Commit today to mentally reminding yourself that only Allah is eternal, and let that change how you value the temporary world.

The First Proof for the Temporality of Bodies
Sanusi presents the first proof for the temporality of bodies using an infinite regress argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first proof demonstrates that bodies must be created by ruling out an eternal body through the impossibility of an infinite regress of conditions.

Core Idea
An eternal body would require an infinite chain of conditions—impossible, so bodies are created.

This lesson presents the first of several proofs for the temporality of bodies. The author, al-Sanusi, uses a conditional argument to show that if a body were eternal, it would depend on an eternal condition, leading to an infinite regress that cannot be sustained. The argument forces the conclusion that bodies must have a beginning.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage from 'المرصد الثاني' to 'وهو المطلوب'. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure.
2
Identify the key conditional
The proof starts with 'if the body is not created (eternal), then the eternal conditioned by it would be more deserving of creation' — understand how this leads to an infinite regress.
The argument is about conditions for emanation.
3
Trace the infinite regress argument
Follow how the author forces an end to the regress at something that must emanate without condition, and how the non-existence of that final entity would entail the non-existence of the Necessary Being, which is impossible.
This shows the original body must be created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد الأول : الأجسام المحدثة أربعة أقسام حادثا وإلا لكان القديم المشروط به أولى بالحدوث بل يكون ذلك الشرط أيضاً قديماً ويعود الكلام فيه وفي صدوره عن الواجب هل هو بشرط أو بغير شرط ويلزم الانتهاء إلى ما يجب صدوره عن الواجب بغير شرط دفعاً للتسلسل في الأمور المترتبة الموجودة معاً فلو عدم هذا الصادر المنتهي إليه عدم الواجب هذا خلف فإذا امتنع عدم هذا الشرط مع امتناع عدم الموجب الواجب امتنع عدم مشروطه أيضاً. وهكذا إلى القديم الذي كلامنا فيه وهو المطلوب. وأما بطلان اللازم فبالاتفاق والدليل أما الاتفاق فلأن الأجسام عند الحكماء منحصرة في الفلكيات وحركاتها واجبة عندهم وفي العنصريات وحركاتها جائزة فلا شيء من الأجسام يمتنع عليه الحركة وأما الدليل فلان الأجسام متساوية في الماهية لتركبها من الجواهر الفردة المتماثلة كما عرفت فيصح على كل من الأجسام من الحيز ما صح على الآخر وما ذلك إلا بخروجه عن حيزه أو نقول : الأجسام إما بسيطة ويجوز على كل جزء منه أي من البسيط ما يصح على الآخر فيصح أن يماس بيساره ما يماسه بيمينه وبالعكس، وما هو إلا بالحركة وإما مركبة من البسائط فيصح على بسائطها من يماسها الآخر وما هو إلا بالحركة وبالجملة فنعلم بالضرورة أن مقولة الوضع غير واجبة للبسائط لأن أجزاءها متحدة في الماهية فيجوز تبدل أوضاعها نظرا إلى طبيعتها وكذا للمركبات لأن تبدل أوضاع البسائط التي فيها يستلزم تبدل أوضاعها و نعلم أيضاً بالضرورة أنه ما من جسم إلا ويمكن للقادر المختار الذي خلقه أن يغير وضعه فيجعل يمينه يساره وبالعكس، وإنكاره مكابرة لا يعتد بها
The Second Observatory - The First Objective: Bodies are created in four categories. It would be created, otherwise the eternal conditioned by it would be more deserving of creation, but rather that condition would also be eternal, and the discussion returns to it and to its emanation from the Necessary: is it with a condition or without a condition? And it is necessary to end at what must emanate from the Necessary without a condition to avoid infinite regress in things that are ordered and exist together. So if this final emanation ceased to exist, the Necessary would cease to exist — this is a contradiction. So if the non-existence of this condition is impossible along with the impossibility of the non-existence of the necessitating necessary, then the non-existence of its conditioned is also impossible. And so on until the eternal which is our subject, and that is what is sought. As for the invalidity of the necessary consequence, it is by consensus and proof. As for the consensus: because bodies according to the philosophers are confined to celestial bodies — their motions are necessary according to them — and to elemental bodies — their motions are contingent — so no body has motion impossible for it. As for the proof: because bodies are equal in essence due to their composition from similar indivisible atoms as you know, so whatever is possible for one body regarding position is possible for the other, and that is only by leaving its position. Or we say: bodies are either simple, and it is permissible for each part of it — i.e., of the simple — what is permissible for the other, so it is permissible for it to touch with its left what it touches with its right and vice versa, and that is only by motion; or composite from simples, and it is permissible for their simples what is permissible for the other, and that is only by motion. In short, we know necessarily that the category of position is not necessary for simples because their parts are identical in essence, so it is permissible for their positions to change considering their nature, and likewise for composites because the change of positions of the simples in them necessitates the change of their positions. And we also know necessarily that there is no body except that the powerful chooser who created it can change its position, making its right its left and vice versa, and its denial is stubbornness not to be considered.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the temporality of bodies and then refutes a potential objection. The author begins by stating the heading for the second observatory and first objective, which is that bodies are divided into four categories regarding creation. The proof itself starts with a conditional: if the body were not created (i.e., eternal), then the eternal conditioned by it would be more deserving of creation, leading to an infinite regress of conditions. The argument forces an end to a condition that must emanate from the Necessary Being without any prior condition, otherwise a contradiction arises: if that final conditioned entity ceased, the Necessary would cease. Since that is impossible, the conditioned must also be impossible to cease, tracing back to the eternal body in question, thus proving it must be created. The author then addresses the objection that the necessary consequence of this proof is false. He argues its invalidity by consensus and proof. The consensus is that philosophers divide bodies into celestial (with necessary motions) and elemental (with contingent motions), so no body has motion impossible for it, contradicting the proof's implication. The proof itself argues that all bodies are equal in essence, composed of similar atoms, so what is possible for one position-wise is possible for another, implying the ability to change position (motion). This applies to simples and composites alike. Additionally, we know necessarily that any body can have its position changed by the powerful chooser, so the necessary consequence is false, and the original proof stands.

Key Terms
حادث
قديم
جسم
كون
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first proof relies on the idea that an eternal body cannot move.
Correction: The proof actually relies on the impossibility of an infinite regress of conditions, not on the impossibility of motion for an eternal body. Motion is addressed separately in the refutation.

Checkpoint
What is the key logical move in the first proof to avoid infinite regress?
A) Postulating that all bodies are composed of atoms.
B) Requiring an endpoint that emanates from the Necessary without condition.
C) Assuming that motion is impossible for eternal bodies.
D) Using consensus among philosophers.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows bodies must be created because an eternal body would entail an infinite regress of conditions, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every aspect of your existence depends on conditions that ultimately trace back to the Necessary Being, where in your life have you been acting as if you are self-sufficient and independent of Allah? What will you change today to acknowledge your dependence?
?
The proof shows that an infinite chain of conditions is impossible. Are there created things—people, possessions, status—that you have elevated in your heart as if they were eternal and independent, forgetting they themselves are conditioned and dependent? How will you recalibrate your reliance?
?
This proof forces you to confront that you are not self-existent. What does that mean for how you view your own existence and purpose? How will you remind yourself daily of your utter dependence on the Necessary Being in your worship and thoughts?

Refutation of the Necessary Consequence: Bodies Can Move
Sanusi refutes the objection that the first proof's necessary consequence (eternal bodies cannot move) is false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Sanusi refutes the objection that the first proof implies an eternal body cannot move, using consensus and a proof from the nature of bodies.

Core Idea
Bodies are equal in essence and can change position, so an eternal body could move, invalidating the objection.

After presenting the first proof for the temporality of bodies, the author addresses a potential objection: that the proof implies an eternal body would be impossible because it would require an eternal condition that cannot change. He refutes this by showing that bodies, by their nature, are capable of positional change, and this is supported by consensus and rational proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read from 'وأما بطلان اللازم' to the end. Notice the two parts: consensus and proof.
2
Understand the consensus argument
The consensus claim is that philosophers divide bodies into celestial (with necessary motions) and elemental (with contingent motions), so no body is absolutely motionless.
This counters the supposed impossibility of motion for an eternal body.
3
Analyze the proof from essence
The proof argues that bodies are equal in essence (composed of similar atoms), so what is possible for one is possible for another regarding position. Since we observe bodies changing position, all bodies can change position, even if eternal.
The author also gives an alternative argument based on simples and composites.
4
Reflect on the final point
The author asserts we know necessarily that a powerful chooser can change any body's position, making denial mere stubbornness.
Thus the objection fails.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why does the consensus among philosophers show that the necessary consequence is false?
A) Because all bodies are composed of atoms.
B) Because celestial bodies have necessary motions and elemental bodies have contingent motions, so no body is motionless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider something in your life that you have considered 'fixed' or unchangeable—whether a sin, a broken relationship, or a despairing heart. The passage says that denying the possibility of change is mere stubbornness (mukābara). Do you truly believe that the powerful chooser (al-qādir al-mukhtār) who created the heavens can change that situation? What would it take for you to stop insisting it's impossible and instead turn to Him in hope?
?
The author argues that bodies can change their position because the Creator can change their positions. What does your own resistance to change reveal about your trust in God's power? In what area of your life are you acting as if God's power is limited, and how can you actively submit to His ability to transform it?
?
The proof relies on the equality of bodies in essence: what is possible for one is possible for another. So if God can change the state of a person in the past (like from disbelief to faith), He can also change you. Has your awareness of God's absolute power ever genuinely moved you to repentance? If not, what is holding you back?

The Second Proof: Avoiding Complications of the First
The author presents a dilemma-based proof for the temporality of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second proof for the temporality of bodies avoids the complications of the first by focusing on the accident of location (kawm) rather than motion and rest.

Core Idea
The second proof simplifies the argument by using an undisputed accident: location.

The first proof for the temporality of bodies required extensive discussions about the nature of motion and rest and whether rest is an existent accident. The second proof, attributed to later scholars, cuts through these complications by focusing on the accident of being in a place (kawm), which is uncontroversially existent. This lesson presents that argument, showing how it confronts the proponent of an eternal body with a decisive dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
The argument sets up a dilemma for anyone claiming an eternal body: either the body has an eternal accident of location, or it has an infinite series of such accidents. Both are shown to be impossible.
This is the core logical structure of the proof.
2
Examine the first horn: eternal kawm
If the body has a kawm not preceded by another, that kawm would have to be eternal, which is impossible as argued elsewhere.
3
Examine the second horn: infinite regress
If every kawm is preceded by another, an infinite regress of kawms arises, which is also impossible via standard arguments like tatbiq and tada'uf.
4
Appreciate the advantages over the first proof
The second proof avoids debates about rest being existent and about the possibility of a body being neither moving nor at rest in eternity.
This shows the elegance of the later formulation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثاني وهو لبعض المتأخرين كالاختصار للمسلك الأول أنه لو وجد جسم قديم لزم إما كون واحد قديم وإما أن يكون قبل كل كون كون آخر لا إلى نهاية والتالي باطل بقسميه أما الملازمة فلأنه لا بد للجسم من كون في حيز لكونه متحيزاً بالذات فإن وجد له كون غير مسبوق بآخر أي بكون آخر لزم القسم الأول لأن ذلك الكون يجب أن يكون ثابتاً للجسم القديم على الاستمرار فيكون قديماً وإلا أي وإن لم يوجد له كون غير مسبوق بآخر لزم القسم الثاني لأن كل كون له فإنه مسبوق يكون آخر فوجب أن يكون قبل كل كون كون لا إلى نهاية إذ على ذلك التقدير الذي نحن فيه لو وجد كون لا كون قبله لزم خلو الجسم عن الكون وأنت خبير بأن القسم الثاني لا يحتاج إلى هذا البيان لأنه إذا لم يوجد له كون غير مسبوق بآخر كان كل كون له مسبوقاً بكون قبله لا إلى نهاية إنما المحتاج إلى البيان هو القسم الأول، بأن يقال : ذلك الكون الذي ليس مسبوقاً أن يكون مستمراً أزلاً وإلا لزم خلو الجسم عن الكون، نعم لو قيل : إن وجد له كون قديم فهو القسم الأول، وإلا فلا بد أن يكون قبل كل كون كون آخر إذ لو وجد له كون لا كون قبله لزم خلو الجسم عن الكون لانتظم الكلام . وأما بطلان التالي فأما القسم الأول وهو قدم الكون فبمثل ما بينا به حدوث السكون، وأما القسم الثاني وهو تعاقب الأكوان إلى ما لا نهاية له فبالتطبيق وطريقة التضايف وغيرهما من أدلة بطلان التسلسل . ولا يخفى عليك أن في هذا المسلك طرحاً لمؤونات كثيرة كانت في المسلك الأول من بيان كون السكون وجودياً إذ قد اختلف فيه فذهب الحكماء إلى أنه عدم الحركة عما من شأنه الحركة فيجوز حينئذ زواله لأن إعدام الحوادث تزول بوجوداتها مع كونها أزلية فإن الكون الذي ذكر في هذا المسلك لا شك في أنه وجودي بل خلاف ومن بيان أن الجسم لا يخلو عن الحركة والسكون فإن لقائل أن يقول : هو في الأزل لا متحرك ولا ساكن لأن كلاً منهما يقتضي المسبوقية بالغير فلا يصح اتصافه بشيء منهما في الأزل ومن سقوط قولهم : السابقية والمسبوقية في الحركة بالفرض إذ لا أجزاء لها إلا بالوهم وفي الخارج هو أي الحركة كون واحد مستمر بين المبدأ والمنتهى لما مر من أن الحركة تطلق على الأمر الممتد ولا وجود له في الخارج بل يمتنع وجوده فيه وعلى الأمر المستمر الموجود الذي لا انقسام له في مأخذ الحركة وهو الذي يدعي أنه قديم لا المعنى الأول، فتأمل
The second way – it is by some later scholars as a summary of the first way: that if an eternal body existed, it would necessitate either a single eternal kawm (accident of being in a place) or that before every kawm there is another kawm ad infinitum. The consequent is false in both divisions. As for the implication: because a body necessarily has a kawm in a place due to being essentially located. If it has a kawm not preceded by another, i.e., by another kawm, the first division follows because that kawm must be permanently attached to the eternal body and thus be eternal. Otherwise, i.e., if it does not have a kawm not preceded by another, the second division follows because every kawm it has is preceded by another kawm, so there must be before every kawm a kawm ad infinitum. For on that hypothesis under discussion, if there were a kawm with no kawm before it, the body would be devoid of a kawm. And you know that the second division does not need this explanation because if it does not have a kawm not preceded by another, then every kawm it has is preceded by a kawm before it ad infinitum. Only the first division needs explanation, by saying: that kawm which is not preceded must be perpetually eternal, otherwise the body would be devoid of a kawm. Indeed, if it is said: if it has an eternal kawm, that is the first division; otherwise, there must be before every kawm another kawm, because if it had a kawm with no kawm before it, the body would be devoid of a kawm, for the argument to be coherent. As for the invalidity of the consequent: as for the first division, i.e., the eternity of the kawm, it is by the same method we proved the temporality of rest. As for the second division, i.e., the succession of kawms ad infinitum, it is by the method of application (tatbiq) and the method of mutual relation (tada'uf) and other proofs of the impossibility of infinite regress. And it is not hidden from you that this way discards many burdens that were in the first way: namely, the demonstration that rest is an existent (wujudi) – for there is disagreement about it, the philosophers holding that it is the absence of motion in that which is capable of motion, so it is permissible for it to cease because the non-existences of accidents cease with their existences despite being eternal – whereas the kawm mentioned in this way is undoubtedly existent, indeed it is the opposite; and the demonstration that a body is never devoid of motion and rest – for someone might say: in eternity it is neither moving nor at rest because each of them requires being preceded by another, so it cannot be described by either in eternity; and the collapse of their saying: priority and posteriority in motion are by assumption, because it has no parts except in estimation, while in external reality it – i.e., motion – is a single continuous kawm between the beginning and the end, because it has passed that motion is applied to the extended thing which has no existence externally, rather its existence is impossible there, and to the continuous existent thing which has no division in the locus of motion, and it is that which they claim is eternal, not the first meaning. So reflect.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for the temporality of bodies, which is a summary of the first but avoids certain complications. The argument begins by assuming an eternal body. It then claims that such a body must have a 'kawm' (the accident of being in a place) because it is essentially located. Two possibilities arise: either the body has a kawm that is not preceded by another (making that kawm eternal, which is impossible), or every kawm is preceded by another, leading to an infinite regress of kawms. Both are shown to be false. The first division is refuted by the same method used to prove the temporality of rest, and the second by standard arguments against infinite regress (tatbiq and tada'uf). The author then notes the advantages of this argument over the first: it avoids the need to prove that rest is an existent accident (since kawm is undoubtedly existent), it avoids the objection that in eternity a body might be neither moving nor at rest, and it sidesteps the debate over motion being a continuous existent vs. an extended mental construct. The passage ends with an encouragement to reflect.

Misconception: The second proof is entirely different from the first proof.
Correction: It is a summary of the first proof, avoiding its complications but sharing the same underlying logic.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The second proof simplifies the argument for the temporality of bodies by using the accident of location, which is uncontroversially existent, thus avoiding the complexities of motion and rest.

The author ends with 'So reflect.' Have you honestly reflected, or are you merely collecting arguments? What specific change in your worldview or daily life will result from understanding this proof?
?
This proof simplifies complex issues by focusing on location. Are there areas in your faith where you seek complexity to avoid the simple, demanding truth? What is one simple truth you have been avoiding?

Al-Razi's Argument from Motion or Rest in Eternity
Al-Razi's argument from motion or rest in eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's argument that an eternal body would be either moving or at rest in eternity, both of which are impossible, thus proving the body must be created.

Core Idea
An eternal body cannot be moving or at rest—so it must be created.

Imam al-Razi presents a streamlined version of the first proof for the temporality of bodies. Instead of multiple premises, he focuses on a simple dichotomy: an eternal body would have to be either moving or at rest in eternity. Both options lead to contradictions. This lesson unpacks that concise argument and shows why it reinforces the Ash'arite position.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the author's note that this proof is derived from the first one, with fewer burdens.
The passage is brief; let the argument's logic sink in.
2
Identify the core dichotomy
The argument states that an eternal body must be either moving (متحرك) or at rest (ساكن) in eternity. Identify these two states and consider why each is impossible.
This is the key structure of the proof.
3
Understand the impossibility of both
If moving in eternity, change would occur in an eternal being, which is impossible. If at rest, that would imply permanence requiring time, also impossible. Both are false, so the body cannot be eternal.
The commentary explains this in more detail.
4
Reflect on the source of the proof
Note that al-Razi himself attributed this to earlier sources, and the author mentions it is taken from the first proof. Consider why this version is simpler.
This shows the interconnectedness of kalam arguments.
5
Complete the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to test your understanding of the argument's core dichotomy.
The answer is B: either moving or at rest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثالث للإمام الرازي : ذكره في المحصل ونسبه الآمدي إلى بعض المتأخرين من الأشاعرة وهو أيضاً مأخوذ من المسلك الأول والمؤونات التي كانت فيه باقية هاهنا بحالها سوى قليل منها كما لا يخفى وتقريره أنه لو وجد جسم قديم لكان في الأزل إما متحركاً أو ساكناً والتالي باطل بقسميه وأنت بمعرفة بيانه بعد ما قررناه في المسلكين السابقين خبير فلا نشتغل حذفاً للمؤونة
The third way of Imam al-Razi: He mentioned it in al-Muhassal, and al-Amidi attributed it to some later Ash'arites. It is also taken from the first way, and the burdens that were in it remain here as they were except for a few, as is not hidden. Its formulation is that if an eternal body existed, it would be in eternity either moving or at rest, and the consequent is false in both divisions. And you, being knowledgeable of its explanation after what we established in the two previous ways, are expert, so we do not engage in it to save effort.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the temporality of bodies, attributed to Imam al-Razi and mentioned in his book al-Muhassal, also attributed by al-Amidi to later Ash'arites. The argument is derived from the first proof, but with fewer complexities. It states that if a body were eternal, then in eternity it would have to be either moving or at rest. Both options are impossible: if moving, it would imply change in an eternal being; if at rest, it would imply permanence that requires time. Since both are false, the body must be created. The author notes that the explanation is similar to the previous two proofs and thus does not elaborate further to save effort.

?

The argument refrains from elaboration to 'save effort'—yet you have the opportunity to reflect. In what area of your faith have you been relying on shortcuts instead of deep understanding? Commit to studying one proof of tawheed thoroughly this week.

The First Two Proofs for the Origination of Bodies
The author enumerates two possibilities for bodies and refutes each to prove origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the two main proofs in kalām establish that bodies are originated (ḥādith) by refuting the possibilities of a beginningless attribute or an infinite regress.

Core Idea
The body must have a beginning: an eternal attribute or infinite regress is impossible.

The kalām theologians devised multiple proofs for the origination of bodies. This passage presents two concise proofs that avoid earlier complications. By examining the two possibilities—either a body possesses a beginningless attribute or an endless chain of attributes—the argument demonstrates that neither can hold, forcing the conclusion that bodies are originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall argument. Note the two divisions presented at the beginning.
The author uses the term 'kawn' for attribute, which is a key term.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage divides bodies into two cases: either it has a beginningless attribute (first division) or it must have an infinite regress of attributes (second division). Understand why these are exhaustive.
The first case would mean the body contains an eternal accident, which is impossible.
3
Understand the refutations
For the first division, the refutation is similar to the proof against eternal rest. For the second, it uses arguments against infinite regress (taṭbīq and taḍāyuf). Read the commentary to see how each is refuted.
These refutations rely on earlier established principles.
4
Reflect on the advantages
The author notes that this new pair of proofs avoids many problems of the earlier 'first path', such as proving that rest is existent or that the body must always be in motion or rest. Consider why this is an improvement.
This shows the development of kalām argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجد له كون قديم فهو القسم الأول، وإلا فلا بد أن يكون قبل كل كون كون آخر إذ لو وجد له كون لا كون قبله لزم خلو الجسم عن الكون لانتظم الكلام. وأما بطلان التالي فأما القسم الأول وهو قدم الكون فبمثل ما بينا به حدوث السكون، وأما القسم الثاني وهو تعاقب الأكوان إلى ما لا نهاية له فبالتطبيق وطريقة التضايف وغيرهما من أدلة بطلان التسلسل. ولا يخفى عليك أن في هذا المسلك طرحاً لمؤونات كثيرة كانت في المسلك الأول من بيان كون السكون وجودياً إذ قد اختلف فيه فذهب الحكماء إلى أنه عدم الحركة عما من شأنه الحركة فيجوز حينئذ زواله لأن إعدام الحوادث تزول بوجوداتها مع كونها أزلية فإن الكون الذي ذكر في هذا المسلك لا شك في أنه وجودي بل خلاف ومن بيان أن الجسم لا يخلو عن الحركة والسكون فإن لقائل أن يقول: هو في الأزل لا متحرك ولا ساكن لأن كلاً منهما يقتضي المسبوقية بالغير فلا يصح اتصافه بشيء منهما في الأزل ومن سقوط قولهم: السابقية والمسبوقية في الحركة بالفرض إذ لا أجزاء لها إلا بالوهم وفي الخارج هو أي الحركة كون واحد مستمر بين المبدأ والمنتهى لما مر من أن الحركة تطلق على الأمر الممتد ولا وجود له في الخارج بل يمتنع وجوده فيه وعلى الأمر المستمر الموجود الذي لا انقسام له في مأخذ الحركة وهو الذي يدعي أنه قديم لا المعنى الأول، فتأمل
If a body has a beginningless attribute (kawn qadīm), then it is the first division; otherwise it must be that before every attribute there is another attribute, for if it had an attribute with no attribute before it, the body would be devoid of the attribute, as the statement is arranged. And as for the refutation of the consequent: as for the first division, which is the eternity of the attribute, it is by the same kind as we demonstrated the origination of rest (sukūn); and as for the second division, which is the succession of attributes indefinitely, it is by the method of correlation (taṭbīq) and the method of mutual relation (taḍāyuf) and other proofs of the invalidity of the infinite regress (tasalsul). And it is not hidden from you that in this path there is a casting aside of many burdens that were in the first path, such as the demonstration that rest (sukūn) is existent—for there is disagreement about it, the philosophers (al-ḥukamā') holding that it is the absence of motion from what is capable of motion, so its disappearance is permissible at that time because the non-existence of accidents is removed by their existences while they are eternal—for the attribute (kawn) mentioned in this path is undoubtedly existent, rather it is different; and such as the demonstration that the body is never free of motion and rest, for one might say: in pre-eternity it is neither moving nor at rest because each of them requires being preceded by another, so it is not correct to be described by either of them in pre-eternity; and such as the falling away of their statement: priority and being preceded in motion are by supposition, for it has no parts except in the imagination (wahm), and in the external world it, i.e. motion, is a single continuous attribute between the beginning and the end, because it has been mentioned that motion is applied to the extended thing, which does not exist in the external world, rather its existence is impossible in it, and to the continuous existent thing which has no division in the source of motion, and it is what they claim is eternal, not the first meaning. So reflect.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage presents the first two proofs (maslakān) for the origination of bodies. The author begins by dividing bodies into two cases: either a body has a beginningless attribute (kawn qadīm) or it does not. If it does, that attribute is eternal, which is impossible because it would be an eternal accident. If it does not, then an infinite regress of attributes would be required, which is also impossible due to the arguments against regress (method of correlation and mutual relation). The author then notes that this pair of proofs avoids many difficulties of an earlier proof (the first path mentioned in a previous discussion). Those difficulties included the need to prove that rest is existent (since some philosophers considered it a mere absence of motion) and the need to argue that a body is never free of motion or rest. The author points out that in this new proof, the attribute (kawn) is clearly existent, and there is no need to discuss whether the body in pre-eternity is moving or at rest because both options require a predecessor. Furthermore, the notion of priority in motion is by supposition; in reality, motion is a single continuous attribute. Thus the two possibilities are refuted, establishing that bodies must have a beginning.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first two proofs require that a body be empty of all attributes in pre-eternity.
Correction: The proofs only address the impossibility of a beginningless attribute or infinite regress; they do not claim the body is devoid of attributes. The attribute (kawn) is accepted as existent.

Checkpoint
What are the two possibilities considered for a body in the first two proofs?
A) Either it has a beginningless attribute or it has a succession of attributes indefinitely.
B) Either it is composed of atoms or it is composed of matter and form.
C) Either it is necessarily existent or it is possible.
D) Either it is at rest or it is in motion in pre-eternity.

Key Takeaway
The two proofs show that a body cannot have a beginningless attribute nor an infinite regress of attributes, so it must have a beginning and therefore be originated (ḥādith).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read a logical proof that your very existence is not eternal—you have a beginning. Does knowing this intellectually truly change the way you live today, or do you still act as if you will never cease to exist? What one habit would be different if you genuinely felt the reality of your contingency?
?
The author invites you to 'reflect' (tā'ammal). When was the last time you sat still and reflected on the signs of creation as indicators of the Creator, not through emotion alone but through careful reasoning? What stops you from doing that regularly?
?
This passage sets aside many burdens and simplifies the proof. In your spiritual journey, are you willing to let go of unnecessary complexities and hold firmly to clear, rational proofs of your dependence on Allah? What complexities are you holding onto that keep you from true submission?

The Sixth Path: Bodies as Loci for Accidents
Defines the sixth path and its empirical basis for proving bodily origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the sixth path proves the origination of bodies by establishing that bodies are loci for originated accidents, and why this path is considered in the same ruling as the first two.

Core Idea
Bodies are originated because they host accidents, and the eternal cannot host accidents.

The author now presents the sixth path for demonstrating the origination of bodies. This path builds on the observation that bodies undergo changes—movements, colors, shapes—which are originated events. Since the eternal cannot be subject to such changes, bodies must be originated. The author also clarifies that this path is essentially equivalent to the first two, avoiding unnecessary multiplication of arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The passage begins by noting that the previous paths only prove origination of bodies and their attributes, and require negation of incorporeal entities to generalize. The author then explains why he didn't include the third path and clarifies that the sixth is like the first two.
This sets up that the sixth path is not a new independent proof but reinforces the same conclusion.
2
Read the definition of the sixth path
Read the sentence 'المسلك السادس: الجسم يقوم به الحادث' and its translation. Reflect on what it means for a body to be that to which the originated accident subsists.
The term 'يقوم به' indicates that accidents inhere in the body.
3
Observe the empirical evidence
The author provides observational examples: movements, lights, colors, and shapes are originated and we see them in bodies. This empirical evidence grounds the proof.
This shows that the proof relies on direct experience.
4
Connect to the nature of the eternal
The passage concludes by stating that nothing eternal can be a locus for originated things, which will be proven later. Thus, bodies cannot be eternal because they host originated accidents.
This establishes the logical conclusion that bodies are originated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذلك فإنهما لا يعطيان إلا حدوث الأجسام وصفاتها ويحتاج في تعميمها إلى نفي المجردات ولم يتعرض للمسلك الثالث لأنه جعله عين الأول لبقاء المؤونات وأما السادس فهو في حكم الأولين بلا اشتباه. المسلك السادس: الجسم يقوم به الحادث وهو ضروري لما نشاهده من حدوث الحركات القائمة به وتجدد الأعراض الحالة فيه كالأضواء والألوان والأشكال وغيرها، ولا شيء من القديم لما سنبرهن عليه في الإلهيات من أن القديم لا يكون محلا للحوادث.
Likewise, they only give the origination of bodies and their attributes, and in generalizing them one needs to negate incorporeal entities. He did not address the third path because he considered it identical to the first due to remaining difficulties. As for the sixth, it is in the same ruling as the first two without doubt. The sixth path: The body is that to which the originated [accident] subsists, and it is necessary due to what we observe of the origination of movements subsisting in it and the renewal of accidents occurring in it such as lights, colors, shapes, and others. And nothing of the eternal [can be so] because we will prove in the divine matters that the eternal cannot be a locus for originated things.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth path (المسلك السادس) for proving the origination of bodies. The author first notes that the previous paths only prove the origination of bodies and their attributes, and to generalize they need to negate incorporeal substances. He then clarifies that the sixth path is essentially the same ruling as the first two. The sixth path itself states that a body is that to which the originated accident subsists; this is necessary because we observe originated movements and accidents like lights, colors, shapes. The eternal cannot have such accidents, as will be proven in the section on divine attributes. This establishes that bodies are originated because they are loci for originated accidents, and the eternal cannot be such a locus.

Key Terms
المسلك السادس
الحادث
القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sixth path is a completely separate and independent proof from the first two.
Correction: The author explicitly states that the sixth path is 'in the same ruling as the first two without doubt', meaning it is essentially equivalent and not a new independent argument.

Checkpoint
What is the key empirical observation that supports the sixth path?
A) Bodies are composed of atoms.
B) Bodies undergo movement and change of accidents like colors and shapes.
C) Bodies are eternal.
D) Bodies are incorporeal.

Key Takeaway
The sixth path proves bodies are originated because they are observed to be the locus for originated accidents, and the eternal cannot be such a locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body is constantly hosting originated accidents — movements, colors, even your breath. If nothing eternal can host such things, what does that force you to conclude about your own origin and your total dependence on the Creator? Are you living with that awareness every moment?
?
You just learned that the eternal cannot be a locus for originated things. Look at your own life: you are a locus for countless originated changes — thoughts, emotions, physical states. How does that undeniable fact confront you with the reality that you are not self-sufficient? What would it look like to live today in conscious dependence on the One who alone is eternal?
?
If every accident in your body — every heartbeat, every thought — is originated and passing, and you are the locus of those accidents, then you are proof of your own origination. What is stopping you from fully submitting to the Eternal as your Lord and Originator? What would you need to let go of to embrace that?

Refuting the Material Cause Argument for the Eternity of Bodies
Presents and refutes the material cause argument for the eternity of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's material cause argument for the eternity of bodies and the systematic refutation presented by the theologian.

Core Idea
Matter is not eternal; the composition of body from matter and form is denied.

The opponents of Islamic theology often employed arguments based on Greek philosophy to claim the eternity of the universe. One such argument relies on the concept of matter as a material cause. In this passage, the theologian presents the objection and then dismantles it through multiple lines of attack, rejecting the very premises on which it rests.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: first the objection, then the answer with several points.
2
Identify the three refutation points
The answer contains three distinct refutations: denial of composition, denial of matter's eternity, and denial that matter always has form. Find each one in the text.
The first point is the strongest: it questions the very framework of matter and form.
3
Understand the logic behind the refutation
Ask yourself why the theologian denies that matter is eternal even if composition is granted. The argument mentions varying predispositions and essential necessity, which will be refuted later.
This shows the interconnectedness of theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الخصم على القدم بشبه أربع: الأولى وهي مستخرجة من العلة المادية أن يقال: المادة قديمة وإلا احتاجت إلى مادة أخرى، لما عرفت من أن كل حادث مسبوق بالمادة، وتسلسل أي لزم التسلسل في المواد، وإنها أي المادة لا تخلو عن الصورة الجسمية والنوعية أيضا لما تقدم، فيلزم قدم الجسم لكون أجزائه بأسرها قديمة. والجواب: منع تركب الجسم من المادة والصورة، وإن سلمنا ذلك لا نسلم كون المادة قديمة، فإنه أي كونها قديمة يثبت بوجود اختلاف الاستعداد المقرب إلى وجود الحوادث كما سلف، وإنه فرع الإيجاب بالذات وسنبطله بإثبات قدرة الصانع في الموقف الخامس، ولا نسلم أيضا أنها لا تخلو عن الصورة وقد مر ضعف دليله.
The opponent argued for eternity with four [sophistries]: The first, taken from the material cause, is that matter is eternal; otherwise it would need another matter, since every originated thing is preceded by matter, leading to an infinite regress. And matter is never devoid of corporeal and specific form, as previously established, so the body is eternal because all its parts are eternal. The answer: We deny that the body is composed of matter and form. Even if we grant that, we do not concede that matter is eternal, for its eternity is supposedly proven by the existence of varying predispositions bringing about originated things, as previously mentioned, and that is a consequence of essential necessity, which we will disprove by proving the power of the Creator in the fifth station. Also, we do not concede that matter is never devoid of form; the weakness of its proof has already been shown.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection (شبهة الأولى) from the material cause. The opponent argues that matter must be eternal because if it were originated, it would need prior matter, leading to an infinite regress. Also, matter always has form, so the body's parts are eternal, making the body eternal. The answer has several points: (1) Denial that body is composed of matter and form (i.e., the Aristotelian composition is not accepted). (2) Even if we accept composition, we deny that matter is eternal; rather, its supposed eternity is based on varying predispositions (استعداد) which is a consequence of essential necessity (الإيجاب بالذات), which will be refuted later. (3) We also deny that matter is never devoid of form; the argument for that already was shown weak.

Key Terms
العلة المادية
المادة
الصورة
الاستعداد
الإيجاب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Matter is eternal because otherwise an infinite regress of matters would occur.
Correction: The theologian denies the premises: bodies are not composed of matter and form in the Aristotelian sense, and even if they were, matter is not eternal; its supposed eternity is based on unproven assumptions.

Checkpoint
What is the first refutation point against the material cause argument?
A) The body is composed of matter and form.
B) We deny that the body is composed of matter and form.
C) Matter is originated.
D) The body is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The material cause argument fails because its foundational premises—that bodies are composed of matter and form, that matter is eternal, and that matter always has form—are all denied or shown to be unproven.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the origins of the universe, do you unknowingly adopt assumptions from philosophical systems like Aristotle's? What would it cost you to abandon those assumptions if they contradicted revelation?
?
The author denies that bodies are composed of matter and form—a seemingly obvious truth. Are you holding onto any 'obvious' beliefs about the world that you have never critically examined? How would you feel if they were dismantled?
?
This refutation will only be complete when divine power is proven. Are you intellectually restless enough to demand that God's power, not human philosophy, be the ultimate explanation? Or do you prefer conclusions that leave you in control?

Second Objection from Time (Formal Cause) and Its Refutation
Presents and refutes the argument that time is eternal based on formal cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the refutation of the second objection from formal cause distinguishes temporal priority from essential priority to avoid contradiction.

Core Idea
Time's priority is essential, not temporal, so no contradiction arises.

This passage presents a powerful objection to the createdness of the world: if time is not eternal, then its non-existence before existence would involve a temporal priority that leads to a contradiction. The opponent argues that time must be eternal, implying the eternity of motion and body. The refutation skillfully disentangles two types of priority, showing that the priority of time's non-existence is essential, not temporal, thus defusing the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the overall objection and answer.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Notice the opponent's argument: time must be eternal, otherwise temporal priority would cause a contradiction.
3
Analyze the structure
The passage divides into an objection (conditional reasoning) and a two-part answer (denial, then redefinition of priority).
4
Understand the refutation moves
First, temporal priority is denied because it presupposes time. Second, even if priority exists, it is essential, not temporal.
5
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on why this distinction matters for proving the world's createdness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية وقد نسبها الإمام الرازي إلى العلة الصورية أن يقال: الزمان قديم وإلا كان عدمه قبل وجوده قبلية لا يجامع فيها السابق المسبوق وهو السبق الزماني فيكون موجوداً حين ما فرض معدوماً، هذا خلف. وإذا كان الزمان قديماً كانت الحركة التي هو مقدارها قديماً فكذا الجسم الذي هو محل الحركة. والجواب: منع أن التقدم بالزمان، أي لا نسلم تحقق التقدم الزماني فإنه فرع وجود الزمان وهو غير مسلم، وإن سلم تحققه في الجملة فليس تقدم عدم الزمان على وجوده بالزمان حتى يلزم اجتماع النقيضين بل هو كتقدم أجزاء الزمان بعضها على بعض، أعني التقدم بالذات لا بأمر زائد عليها فلا محذور حينئذ.
The second sophistry, attributed by Imam al-Razi to the formal cause, is that time is eternal; otherwise its non-existence before its existence would be a priority that does not involve a preceding and preceded [relationship] — this is temporal priority, so it would be existent at the moment it was assumed non-existent, which is a contradiction. And if time is eternal, then motion, of which time is the measure, is eternal, and likewise the body which is the locus of motion. The answer: We deny temporal priority; we do not concede the reality of temporal priority, for it is a consequence of the existence of time, which is not granted. And even if its reality is granted in general, the priority of time's non-existence over its existence is not temporal, so as to entail the conjunction of opposites; rather, it is like the priority of some parts of time over others — that is, priority by essence, not by something additional, so there is no problem.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection (شبهة الثانية) from the formal cause, attributed to Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. The opponent argues that time must be eternal; otherwise, its non-existence before existence would involve a temporal priority that leads to a contradiction. If time is eternal, then motion (which time measures) is eternal, and the body (locus of motion) is eternal. The answer: We deny that there is a real temporal priority because that presupposes time itself. Even if we grant some kind of priority, it is not temporal priority but essential priority (تقدم بالذات), like the priority of one part of time over another — this does not lead to a contradiction. So the objection fails.

Key Terms
الزمان
التقدم بالزمان
التقدم بالذات
العلة الصورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time must be eternal because its non-existence before existence would involve a temporal priority that leads to contradiction.
Correction: The priority is essential (تقدم بالذات), not temporal, so no contradiction arises.

Checkpoint
How does the answer avoid the contradiction in the argument about time?
A) By denying the existence of time.
B) By asserting that time is originated.
C) By distinguishing temporal priority from essential priority.
D) By accepting the argument.

Key Takeaway
The refutation of the second objection shows that time's priority is essential, not temporal, thus preserving the possibility of time's createdness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you instinctively think of priority in terms of time? When you consider your own existence, do you ever reflect that your dependence on Allah is essential, not temporal—that He is prior to you in essence, not just in time? How would your sense of urgency in worship change if you truly felt that priority today?
?
The objection assumes that if time were created, its non-existence would have to be temporally prior—an assumption you might make about your own habits or beliefs. Can you identify a belief you hold that you think is justified because you've always held it (temporal priority), but perhaps you have never examined whether it is actually prior in essence? Will you test it today?
?
This passage redefines priority to avoid a contradiction. Are there areas in your life where you assume a certain framework (like time) is the only way to understand something, and that leads you to doubt Allah's power? What would it look like to adopt the framework of essential priority in your trust in Allah's decree?
