Third Objection from Efficient Cause and the Answer by Counterexample of Daily Events
Presents the efficient cause argument and its refutation by counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the efficient cause argument for the world's eternity and its refutation through the counterexample of daily originated events.

Core Idea
The strongest objection to temporal creation is refuted by showing it would also make daily events eternal.

In this passage, the opponent presents what they consider their strongest argument—the efficient cause argument for the world's eternity. The author then offers a powerful refutation by using the very same reasoning to argue that if it were valid, it would also entail that everyday events like sunrise and sunset are eternal, which is obviously false. This lesson walks through both the objection and the counterexample.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its reformulation
Start by reading the efficient cause argument in its original and simpler forms. Notice how the opponent tries to prove the world's eternity by analyzing God's agency.
The objection is considered the strongest of three.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The objection forces a choice: either God's agency is eternal (so the world is eternal) or it is originated (leading to infinite regress). Both lead to the world's eternity.
3
Examine the first response: the counterexample
Read the author's first reliable answer: the counterexample of daily events. If the opponent's reasoning were sound, then every daily event would have to be eternal, which is absurd.
This is a classic refutation by showing the argument proves too much.
4
Follow the opponent's evasion and the author's rebuttal
The opponent tries to evade by claiming daily events involve an infinite regress of astronomical conditions. The author rebuts by stating that infinite regress is impossible whether simultaneous or successive, and also turns the tables by applying the same logic to the world's creation.
The passage cuts off during the second reply; only the first response is complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة: وهي العمدة عندهم في إثبات مطلبهم ومأخوذة من العلة المؤثرة أن يقال: فاعلية الفاعل للعالم أي تأثيره فيه وإيجاده إياه قديمة ويلزم منه قدم العالم. بيانه أنه لو كانت فاعليته حادثة مخصوصة بوقت معين لتوقفت على شرط حادث مختص بذلك الوقت، وإلا أي وإن لم تتوقف على شرط كذلك لزم الترجيح بلا مرجح لأن اختصاص حدوث الفاعلية حينئذ بذلك الوقت دون ما قبله وما بعده مع تساوي نسبتها إلى جميع الأوقات تخصيص بلا مخصص، والكلام في ذلك الشرط الحادث واختصاصه بوقت معين كما في الحادث الأول فلا بد له أيضاً من شرط آخر حادث. ويلزم التسلسل في الشروط الحادثة. وإذا كانت فاعليته قديمة كان الأثر قديماً أيضاً إذ لا يتصور تحقق تأثير وإيجاد حقيقي في زمان مع عدم حصول الأثر فيه. وقد تقرر هذه الشبهة بعبارة أخرى أبسط: فيقال ما لا بد منه في الإيجاد إن كان حاصلاً أزلاً كان الإيجاد حاصلاً فيه إذ لو لم يحصل لكان حصوله بعده إما أن يتوقف على شرط حادث فلا يكون ما لا بد منه حاصلاً وهو خلاف المفروض، أو لا يتوقف فيلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح، وإذا كان الإيجاد أزلياً كان وجود الأثر الذي لا يتخلف عنه كذلك. وإن لم يكن جميع ما لا بد منه في الإيجاد حاصلاً في الأزل كان بعضه حادثاً قطعاً فنقل الكلام إليه ونقول: وإن لم يحتج هذا الحادث إلى إيجاد لزم استغناء الحادث عن المؤثر المخصص، وإن احتاج فإما أن يكون جميع ما لا بد منه في إيجاده حاصلا في الأزل فيلزم قدم الحادث، أو لا يكون حاصلاً فبعضه حادث بالضرورة فيلزم التسلسل في الأسباب والمسببات وهو محال. وقد ذكر في الجواب عنه وجوه والذي يصلح للتعويل عليه وجهان: الأول: النقض بالحادث اليومي إذ لا شبهة في وجوده فنقول: فاعلية الفاعل القديم لهذا الحادث قديمة إذ لو كانت حادثة لوقفت على شرط حادث حذراً من الترجيح بلا مرجح، والكلام في هذا الشرط الحادث كما في الأول فتتسلسل الحوادث المترتبة إلى ما لا نهاية له، فلو صح دليلكم لكان الحادث اليومي قديماً. لا يقال: إنه أي الحادث اليومي يستند إلى الحوادث الفلكية من الحركات والاتصالات الكوكبية وكل منها مسبوق بآخر لا إلى نهاية، ومثل هذا التسلسل جائز بخلاف التسلسل في الأمور المترتبة المجتمعة. لأنا نقول: ابتداء الفارق بين صورة النقض ومحل النزاع على الوجه الذي ذكرتموه لا يدفع النقض لأن التسلسل في الأمور التي ضبطها وجود سواء كانت مجتمعة أو متعاقبة محال كما وقفت عليه. وأيضاً فنقول: إذا سلم جواز التسلسل في الحوادث المتعاقبة فلم لا يجوز أن يكون حدوث العالم مشروطا بشرط مسبوق بآخر لا إلى نهاية فيكون حدوث العالم عن المبدأ القديم بتسلسل الحوادث المتعاقبة كما في الحادث اليومي عندكم. فإن قيل: ذلك أي تسلسل الشروط المتعاقبة إنما يتصور فيما له مادة بتزايد استعدادها بتوارد تلك الشروط عليها لقبول الحادث المشروط بتلك الشروط حتى إذا كمل الاستعداد فاض عليها من المبدأ القديم ما هي مستعدة له، وما سوى العالم أي ما هو خارج عنه ليس له مادة حتى يتصور توارد الشروط المعتبرة في حدوث العالم عليها. قلنا: لا نسلم ذلك الذي ذكرتموه من أن الشروط والحوادث المتعاقبة إنما يتصور في الماديات إذ قد تكون تصورات متعاقبة لأمر مجرد عن المادة وتوابعها كل سابق منها شرط للاحق إلى أن تنتهي فيما يزال إلى ما هو شرط أي إلى تصور هو شرط لحدوث
The third sophistry: it is the main one for them in proving their claim, taken from the efficient cause: that the agency of the agent for the world—i.e., His influencing and bringing it into existence—is eternal, and the eternity of the world follows from it. Its explanation: if His agency were originated, specific to a particular time, it would depend on an originated condition specific to that time; otherwise, if it did not depend on such a condition, then preference without a preferrer would follow, because the specification of the origination of agency at that time rather than before or after, given the equal relation to all times, is specification without a specifier. And the discussion about that originated condition and its specification to a particular time is as in the first originated thing, so it too must have another originated condition. Thus infinite regress in originated conditions follows. And if His agency is eternal, then the effect is also eternal, because a real influence and bringing into existence at a time cannot be conceived without the effect occurring in it. This sophistry has been presented in another, simpler wording: it is said that if that which is necessary for bringing into existence is present from eternity, then the bringing into existence is present in it, because if it were not, its occurrence afterwards would either depend on an originated condition—so that which is necessary would not be present, contrary to the assumption—or it would not depend, then preference without a preferrer would follow. And if the bringing into existence is eternal, then the existence of the effect, which does not lag behind it, is likewise. If not all that is necessary for bringing into existence is present from eternity, then some of it is necessarily originated, and the discussion shifts to it. We say: if this originated thing does not need to be brought into existence, then the originated thing would be independent of the specifying efficient cause; if it needs, then either all that is necessary for its bringing into existence is present from eternity, thus the originated thing would be eternal, or it is not present, so some of it is originated necessarily, leading to infinite regress in causes and effects, which is impossible. It has been mentioned in the answer several aspects, and what is reliable are two: the first: the counterexample by the daily originated event, for there is no doubt about its existence. We say: the agency of the eternal agent for this event is eternal, because if it were originated it would depend on an originated condition to avoid preference without a preferrer, and the discussion about this originated condition is as in the first, so an infinite series of ordered originated events would follow. If your argument were valid, the daily event would be eternal. It cannot be said that it—i.e., the daily event—depends on astronomical events such as motions and planetary conjunctions, each preceded by another without end, and this kind of regress is permissible, unlike regress in simultaneous ordered things. For we say: the mere distinction between the form of the counterexample and the subject of dispute in the way you mentioned does not refute the counterexample, because regress in things whose existence is fixed, whether simultaneous or successive, is impossible, as you have established. Also, we say: if the permissibility of regress in successive events is granted, why is it not allowed that the world’s origination be conditioned by a condition preceded by another without end, so that the world’s origination from the eternal principle occurs through a series of successive events, as in the case of the daily event according to you? For if it is said: that—i.e., the series of successive conditions—is only conceivable for something that has matter, whose predisposition increases through the succession of those conditions upon it to receive the event conditioned by those conditions, so that when the predisposition is complete, from the eternal principle flows what it is predisposed for; and what is other than the world—i.e., what is outside it—has no matter so that the succession of conditions considered in the world’s origination upon it could be conceived. We say: we do not concede what you mentioned, that conditions and successive events are only conceivable in material things, because there can be successive concepts for an immaterial thing devoid of matter and its accessories, each preceding being a condition for the subsequent until they end, in what continues, to that which is a condition—i.e., to a concept that is a condition for origination.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection (شبهة الثالثة), which is considered the strongest, based on the efficient cause. The opponent argues that God's agency must be either eternal or originated. If eternal, the world is eternal; if originated, it requires a further condition leading to infinite regress. The author then gives a simpler formulation. The answer offers two valid responses; only the first is fully presented here. The first response is the counterexample of daily events (النقض بالحادث اليومي): if the opponent's reasoning were sound, then every daily event would also be eternal, which is absurd. The opponent might try to evade by saying that daily events are caused by astronomical cycles with an infinite regress, but the author rejects this by stating that any infinite regress in things whose existence is fixed is impossible, whether simultaneous or successive. The author then turns the tables: if the opponent allows infinite regress in successive events for daily events, then the same could apply to the world's creation. The opponent then objects that such regress only applies to material things with predispositions, but the author replies that immaterial things can also have successive conceptual conditions. The passage cuts off at this point.

Key Terms
العلة المؤثرة
الفاعلية
الإيجاد
التسلسل
النقض بالحادث اليومي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer simply rejects infinite regress without further argument.
Correction: The answer actually uses the opponent's own acceptance of regress in daily events to undermine the argument; it does not simply deny regress.

Checkpoint
What is the first valid response to the third objection?
A) Denial of efficient causality.
B) The counterexample of daily originated events.
C) Affirmation of infinite regress.
D) Denial of the necessity of a condition.

Key Takeaway
The efficient cause argument fails because it would imply the eternity of everyday events, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author uses a simple daily event to dismantle a sophisticated philosophical argument. Think of a daily blessing you take for granted. Could it be that your very existence requires a similar refutation of the doubts you hold? Will you pause today to see God's agency in the ordinary?
?
If the opponent's argument were true, even your morning coffee would be eternal. How does the refutation of this argument shift your perspective on the moment-by-moment creation you experience? Will you live today with the awareness that each moment is new creation?

Bodies are Permanent: The Argument from Rational Necessity
Al-Iji presents the Ash'ari proof that bodies are permanent, responds to the objection from sensory illusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari proof that bodies are permanent, based on rational necessity, and how they respond to the objection from sensory illusion.

Core Idea
Rational necessity, not mere sensation, confirms that bodies persist through time.

In this passage, al-Iji presents the Ash'ari position that bodies are permanent (bāqiya), contrary to al-Nazzām's view that they are continuously renewed. The key proof is grounded in rational necessity (ḍarūra 'aqliyya), which we all intuitively recognize. An objection from sensory illusion is raised and decisively refuted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim and opposition
Read the opening line: 'Bodies are permanent, contrary to al-Nazzām.' Understand that this is the thesis being defended.
Al-Nazzām held that bodies are renewed moment by moment like accidents.
2
Understand al-Amidi's argument from rational necessity
Examine al-Amidi's statement: we know by rational necessity that the mountains, earth, and heavens we saw yesterday are the same today. This is a self-evident truth.
Rational necessity means the mind immediately affirms this without proof.
3
Grasp the objection from sensory illusion
The opponent argues: your certainty is only due to sensory permanence, but sensation is unreliable—it also makes accidents appear permanent, yet you deny their permanence.
The objection challenges consistency in using sensory testimony.
4
Follow the Ash'ari response
The response denies that the certainty is merely sensory. Instead, it is rational necessity, which is self-evident and independent of sensation.
The self-evident (badīhī) does not require a basis; it is affirmed by innate disposition (fiṭra).
5
Consider the supporting evidences
If bodies were not permanent, death/life and transformations (e.g., heating, cooling) would be impossible because they require a single subject. This is absurd, so bodies must be permanent.
These are additional rational proofs that reinforce the claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث : الأجسام باقية خلافاً للنظام ( الأجسام باقية خلافاً للنظام فإنه ذهب إلى أنها متجددة آنا فاناً كالأعراض. وقيل : هذا النقل عنه غير معتمد عليه لأنه قال باحتياج الأجسام إلى المؤثر حال البقاء فتوهمت النقلة أنه لا يقول ببقائها ( ومن أصحابنا ) أي ومن الأشاعرة (من ادعى فيه الضرورة أي ،البداهة قال الآمدي : نحن نعلم بالضرورة العقلية أن ما شاهدناه بالأمس من الجبال الراسيات والأرضين والسماوات هو عين ما نشاهده اليوم وكذا نعلم بالاضطرار أن من فاتحناه بالكلام هو عين من ختمناه معه. وإن أولادنا ورفقاءنا الآن الذين كانوا معنا من قبل ( لا يقال : ليس ذلك ) أي جزمنا ببقائها ضرورة ) إلا لبقائها في الحس) فإنه يشهد باستمرار الأجسام ( ولا يصلح) الحس وشهادته بالبقاء ( للتعويل عليه ) والوثوق به ( إذ الأعراض كذلك ) لأن الحسن شاهد ببقائها . ( وقد قلتم : ( أيها الأشاعرة ( بأنها لا تبقى ) زمانين بل هناك أمثال متجددة لم يدرك الحس تفاوتها فحسبها أمراً واحداً مستمراً فكيف تقبلون شهادته في الأجسام دون الأعراض ؟ ( قلنا : ( أي لأنا نقول : ( لا نسلم أن ذلك ) الجزم منا (ليس إلا للبقاء في الحس حتى يتجه عليه ما ذكرتموه ) بل الضرورة العقلية حاصلة ) بلا شبهة ( والضروري ) البديهي ( لا يطلب مستنده بل هو ما يجزم به مجرد الفطرة ) عند تصور الطرفين وملاحظة النسبة فإن ذلك هو معنى البديهي المرادف للأولى ( ومنهم من استدل عليه بأنه لو لم تكن الأجسام باقية لارتفع الموت والحياة ) أي لم يكن أن يقال لموت حي أو حياة ميت لأن محلهما يجب أن يكون واحداً وعلى ذلك التقدير فالجسم حال حياته غير الجسم حال مماته فلا يكونان واردين على موضوع واحد ( و ) لارتفع ( التسخن والتبرد والتسود والتبيض ونظائرها، أي لم يكن القول بالاستحالة أصلا بأنها مشروطة باتحاد المحل ) وكل ذلك باطل بالضرورة ) العقلية
The Third Inquiry: Bodies are permanent, contrary to al-Nazzam. Bodies are permanent, contrary to al-Nazzam, for he held that they are renewed moment by moment like accidents. And it is said: This report about him is not reliable because he said that bodies need an effecter in the state of permanence, so the transmitters assumed that he does not affirm their permanence. And among our companions (i.e., among the Ash'aris) there are those who claimed necessity (i.e., self-evidence) for it. Al-Amidi said: We know by rational necessity that what we saw yesterday of the firm mountains, the earths, and the heavens is identical to what we see today; likewise, we know by necessity that the one we initiated speech with is the same one we concluded with, and that our children and companions now are those who were with us before. It cannot be said: That (i.e., our certainty of their permanence) is only due to their permanence in sensation, for sensation testifies to the continuity of bodies, and sensation and its testimony to permanence are not reliable, because accidents are also such (i.e., sensation testifies to their permanence). And you (Ash'aris) have said that they do not remain for two moments, but rather there are successive similars whose difference sensation does not perceive, so it deems them a single continuous thing; so how do you accept its testimony for bodies but not for accidents? We reply: We do not grant that this certainty of ours is only due to permanence in sensation, such that what you mentioned would apply; rather, rational necessity is present without doubt. And the necessary (i.e., self-evident) does not seek its basis; rather, it is what the mere innate disposition (fitra) affirms upon conceiving the two extremes and observing the relation—that is the meaning of self-evident, synonymous with primary. And some of them adduced evidence for it: if bodies were not permanent, then death and life would be impossible—i.e., one could not say 'death of a living being' or 'life of a dead being' because their locus must be one, but on this assumption the body during life is other than the body during death, so they would not be incident on a single subject; and also heating, cooling, blackening, whitening, and the like would be impossible—i.e., the very concept of transformation would not exist because it is conditioned by unity of locus. And all of this is void by rational necessity.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari position that bodies are permanent, contrary to al-Nazzam who held that bodies are renewed moment by moment. It begins by noting that some doubt the report that al-Nazzam denied permanence, but the main argument follows. The author (al-Iji or al-Jurjani in the Sharh) quotes al-Amidi: we know by rational necessity that the same mountains, earth, and heavens persist—our certainty is not merely sensory. An objection is raised: sensory permanence is unreliable because accidents also appear permanent to the senses, yet you (Ash'aris) deny their permanence. The response is that our certainty is not from the senses but from rational necessity, which is self-evident and does not depend on proofs. Supporting evidences: if bodies were not permanent, then bringing death to a living being would be meaningless because the subject changes; likewise, transformations like heating and cooling require a single subject. Thus the permanence of bodies is known to reason.

Key Terms
الأجسام (al-ajsām, bodies)
بقاء (baqā', permanence)
النظام (al-Nazzām)
الضرورة العقلية (al-ḍarūra al-‘aqliyya, rational necessity)
البديهي (al-badīhī, self-evident)
الفطرة (al-fiṭra, innate disposition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ari proof relies solely on sensory observation.
Correction: The text explicitly distinguishes between sensory and rational necessity, stating that the certainty comes from rational necessity (ḍarūra 'aqliyya), not from the senses.

Checkpoint
According to al-Amidi, our knowledge that bodies are permanent comes from:
A) Sensory perception alone
B) Rational necessity (al-ḍarūra al-‘aqliyya)
C) Scriptural evidence
D) Consensus of philosophers

Key Takeaway
The permanence of bodies is known by rational necessity, not merely by sensory perception, and this is self-evident to the innate human disposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The permanence of bodies is self-evident to your fitra—but do you live as if your body will not decay? When you look in the mirror, do you see a temporary vessel or an amanah that will be questioned? What will you change today to treat your body as the trust that it is?
?
You are certain that mountains yesterday are the same mountains today, yet you hesitate when affirming Allah's attributes are eternal. Where in your heart is the doubt, and what step will you take this week to replace it with certainty?
?
If rational necessity is present without doubt, why do you entertain doubts about the resurrection of these same bodies on the Day of Judgment? What verse or proof will you memorize and repeat until your fitra submits?

Weakness of the Motion/Rest Proof and Its Generalization Attempts
The author critiques a proof based on motion/rest and its generalizations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proof from motion and rest is considered weak and why attempts to generalize it by analogy are even weaker.

Core Idea
A proof that works for one type of accident does not automatically apply to all.

The author turns to a specific proof used by some theologians to argue that a body cannot be devoid of all accidents. This proof focuses on motion and rest, but as we will see, it only covers a limited case. The author then critiques attempts to extend this proof to all accidents by analogy, showing why such generalizations are flawed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the structure. Notice how the author first presents the proof, then critiques it, then addresses generalizations.
2
Identify the author's critique of the motion/rest proof
The author says the proof is weak because the claim is general (every accident with its opposite) but the proof only covers one pair. Find this point in the text.
3
Recognize the limited utility of the proof
The author notes that despite its weakness, the proof is useful against the Baghdadis and Salihiyya. Understand why.
This shows that a weak argument can still refute certain positions.
4
Examine the two attempted generalizations
The passage presents two attempts to generalize the proof by analogy. The first tries to extend from modes to all accidents, the second uses agreement on impossibility after characterization to argue impossibility before. The author dismisses both due to lack of commonality.
5
Compare the original proof to the generalizations
The original at least proves something for some accidents, while the generalizations prove nothing. This is why they are weaker.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من احتج عليه ) أي على امتناع الخلو ( بامتناع خلوه عن الحركة والسكون كما مر وهو ضعيف لأن الدعوى عامة في كل عرض مع ضده (وهذا) الاحتجاج ( لا تعميم فيه ورب عرض) سوى الحركة والسكون ( يخلو الجسم عنه وعن ضده فإن الهواء خال عن الألوان والطعوم وأضدادهما، نعم يصلح رداً على البغدادية حيث جوزوا الخلو عن الأكوان وعلى الصالحية حيث جوزوا الخلو عن الجميع فيما لا يزال ( وأما قياس البعض على البعض و ( قياس ) ما قيل الاتصاف بما بعد وبالعكس فأضعف من ذلك الضعيف يعني أن بعضهم حاول التعميم في الاحتجاج المذكور . فقال : لما ثبت امتناع الخلو عن الأكوان ثبت امتناعه الأعراض بالقياس عليها، وهو فاسد جداً فساداً ظاهراً إذ لا جامع فيه أصلاً وبعضهم أراد إثبات المدعى ، فقال : اتفقت الأشاعرة والمعتزلة على امتناع الخلو بعد الاتصاف وذلك لإجراء العادة الله من تعالى بخلق المثل أو الضد بعده عند الأشعري وامتناع زوال العرض إلا بطريان ضده عند المعتزلي فكذا يمتنع الخلو قبله قياساً عليه. وهو أيضاً خال عن الجامع مع ظهور الفارق وإنما كانا أضعف من التمسك بالحركة والسكون لأنه يثبت بعضا من المطلوب بخلافها
And among them is one who argued against it) i.e., the impossibility of being devoid (by the impossibility of being devoid of motion and rest as has passed, and it is weak because the claim is general for every accident with its opposite. (And this) argument (has no generalization, and perhaps an accident) other than motion and rest (the body is devoid of it and its opposite, for air is devoid of colors and tastes and their opposites. Yes, it is suitable as a refutation against the Baghdadis where they permitted being devoid of the modes, and against the Salihiyya where they permitted being devoid of all in what continues. (As for analogy of some upon others and (analogy) of what is said of being characterized by what comes after and before, it is weaker than that weak one, meaning that some tried to generalize in the mentioned argument. They said: Since the impossibility of being devoid of the modes is established, the impossibility of being devoid of accidents is established by analogy to them, and it is very corrupt with obvious corruption, for there is no commonality in it at all. And some wanted to prove the claim, so they said: The Ash'aris and Mu'tazila agreed on the impossibility of being devoid after being characterized, due to God's (exalted) continuous custom of creating the like or the opposite after it according to al-Ash'ari, and the impossibility of the removal of an accident except by the occurrence of its opposite according to the Mu'tazili, so likewise being devoid before it is impossible by analogy to it. And it also lacks commonality with the appearance of difference, and they are weaker than clinging to motion and rest because that establishes part of what is sought, unlike them.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage critiques a proof that attempts to show the impossibility of a body being devoid of all accidents by focusing on motion and rest. The author notes that such a proof is weak because the claim is general for every accident with its opposite, but the motion/rest proof only covers one pair. There may be accidents like colors and tastes that a body (e.g., air) can be devoid of along with their opposites. However, this specific proof is useful against the Baghdadis (who permit being devoid of modes) and the Salihiyya (who permit being devoid of all in the future). The author then attacks attempts to generalize this proof by analogy, calling them even weaker. One generalization claims that because being devoid of modes is impossible, it follows for all accidents—but there is no common ground. Another cites the consensus that after having an accident, the body cannot be devoid of it or its opposite, based on divine custom or the Mu'tazilite principle of removal only by opposite, and analogizes that the same applies before the accident. But this also lacks commonality and has clear differences. The author concludes that these are weaker than the original motion/rest proof because that proof at least establishes part of what is wanted (i.e., impossibility for some accidents), while these generalizations prove nothing.

Key Terms
حركة (motion)
سكون (rest)
عرض (accident)
ضد (opposite)
قياس (analogy)
العادة (custom)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The motion/rest proof is completely useless.
Correction: It is useful against specific groups (Baghdadis, Salihiyya) but does not prove the general claim.

Checkpoint
Why is the proof based on motion and rest considered weak by the author?
A) It relies on a false premise
B) The claim is general but the proof only covers one pair of accidents
C) The Baghdadis rejected it
D) It was invented by al-Nazzam

Key Takeaway
A proof that only covers one type of accident cannot be generalized to all accidents without a valid common ground.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find a proof convincing but limited, do you resist the temptation to overapply it? Reflect on a time you might have generalized a truth beyond its proper scope. What would it cost you to admit the limitation?
?
The author shows that even a weak argument can have some utility (refuting certain sects). Are you quick to discard arguments that are not fully airtight? Where in your faith or reasoning could a 'weak but useful' argument serve a valid purpose?
?
Analogy fails here because of a missing common ground. In your own life, where have you drawn analogies between situations that seemed similar but were fundamentally different? How can you train yourself to check for genuine commonality before concluding?

The Second Proof: Infinite Knowledges Requiring Infinite Opposites
The second proof from infinite knowledges to infinite opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proof constructs a reductio ad absurdum: if a body cannot be devoid of accidents and their opposites, then infinite knowledges lead to infinite opposite attributes in a finite human.

Core Idea
Infinite known things would force infinite opposite attributes in a finite being.

This lesson presents the second proof used by those who argue that a body cannot be devoid of accidents. It begins by noting that God can create knowledge of any known thing, and since known things are infinite, the corresponding knowledges would also be infinite. But human knowledge is finite, so if the body cannot be empty of accidents and their opposites, then for each absent knowledge its opposite must be present, resulting in infinite attributes—an impossibility. The same reasoning applies to powers and volitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof structure
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice how the proof moves from 'every known thing can be known' to 'infinite knowledges' to 'finite human' to 'infinite opposites'.
The key move is the assumption that a body cannot be devoid of an accident and its opposite.
2
Identify the premises
List the premises: (1) known things are infinite, (2) each known thing can have a separate knowledge, (3) human knowledge is finite, (4) a body cannot be devoid of an accident and its opposite.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that the human would have infinite opposite attributes, which is impossible. Hence the original assumption (that a body cannot be devoid of accidents) must be false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Apply to powers and volitions
Note that the proof also extends to things within one's power (maqdurāt) and volitions (irādāt), showing the principle is general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثاني : ما من معلوم إلا ويمكن أن يخلق الله تعالى في العبد علماً .به والمعلومات) أي المفهومات التي يمكن أن يتعلق العلم بها ( في نفسها غير متناهية لشمولها الواجب والممكنات والممتنعات فكذا العلوم المتعلقة بها غير متناهية ) والحاصل من تلك العلوم ( للعبد متناه ) لاستحالة وجود ما لا يتناهى ) فإن انتفى ) والظاهر أن يقال : فقد انتفى عنه علوم غير متناهية فكان يجب ) على تقدير امتناع الخلو عن العرض وضده ( أن يقوم به بإزاء كل علم منتف عنه ضد له (فيلزم حينئذ (قيام صفات غير متناهية ) بالعبد، (وكذا الحال ( في المقدورات (ونحوها كالمرادات . ( وأنه محال) لما عرفت ) والجواب أن المنتفي عن العبد هو ( تعلق العلم بما لا يتناهى من المعلومات ( وأنه ( أي ذلك التعلق ( ليس بعرض بل هو أمر اعتباري ( وهذا ) الإلزام الذي ذكرتموه ( إنما يلزم من يحوج كل معلوم إلى علم على حده ويجعله مع أمراً موجوداً لا نفس التعلق الاعتباري ) ونحن لا نقول به بل يجوز أن يتعلق علم واحد بمعلومات متعددة أو نجعله نفس التعلق لا صفة موجودة . ( وأجاب الأستاذ أبو إسحاق بناء على أصله من تضاد العلوم (المتعددة وإن كانت مختلفة لا متماثلة أن ) أي بأن (ضد العلوم (المنتفية التي لا تتناهى ) هو العلم الحاصل سواء كان متعدداً أو واحداً فلا محذور . ( وألزم ) الأستاذ على أصله ( امتناع اجتماع علمين) مطلقاً محل واحد لكونهما متضادين عنده فالتزمه وزعم أن لكل علم محلا غير ما للآخر فلا يجتمع علمان في محل واحد أصلاً ( وأجاب ابن فورك) فقال: المعلومات وإن كانت غير متناهية فالإنسان لا يقبل منها إلا علوما متناهية لامتناع وجود ما لا يتناهى مطلقاً وإذا لم يقبل ما لا يتناهى من العلوم لم يلزم على تقدير خلوه من العلوم التي لا تتناهي أن يتصف بأضداد غير متناهية لأن قيام الضد إنما يكون بدل ما كان المحل قابلاً له قال الآمدي وهذا أشد من جواب الأستاذ . قال المصنف : ( وإنما يصح ) هذا الجواب ( لو امتنع وجود ما لا يتناهى بدلاً كما وجوده (معاً لكنه لم يثبت، وأجيب عنه : بأن اللازم حينئذ اتصاف العبد بصفات من القلب[؟] يمتنع
The second aspect: There is no known thing except that God (exalted) could create in the servant knowledge of it. And the known things) i.e., the concepts that knowledge can relate to (are in themselves infinite due to their encompassing the necessary, the possible, and the impossible. So likewise the knowledges related to them are infinite. And the sum of those knowledges (for the servant is finite) due to the impossibility of existence of what is infinite. So if it is absent) (the apparent thing to say: he is devoid of infinite knowledges. So it would be necessary, (on the assumption of the impossibility of being devoid of an accident and its opposite) that in exchange for every knowledge that is absent, its opposite should subsist in him. (Then it would follow) (the subsistence of infinite attributes) in the servant. (And likewise the case) (in the things that are within his power, and similar things like volitions). (And that is impossible) as you know. (And the answer: What is absent from the servant is) (the relation of knowledge to infinite known things (and that)) (i.e., that relation is not an accident but an i'tibari (relational) thing. (And this) objection that you have mentioned (only applies to one who necessitates for every known thing a separate knowledge and makes it an existent attribute, not the mere i'tibari relation). And we do not say that; rather, it is permissible for one knowledge to relate to multiple known things, or we make it the very relation, not an existent attribute. (And al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq answered based on his principle of the opposition of knowledges, (that) (the opposite of the absent knowledges that are infinite) is the knowledge that is obtained, whether it is multiple or one, so there is no problem. (And al-Ustadh was compelled by his principle) (to assert the impossibility of two knowledges coming together) absolutely in one locus, because they are opposites according to him, so he affirmed it and claimed that every knowledge has a locus other than that of the other, so two knowledges never come together in one locus at all. (And Ibn Furak answered) and said: Even if the known things are infinite, man only accepts from them finite knowledges due to the impossibility of the existence of what is infinite absolutely. And if he does not accept infinite knowledges, then on the assumption of his being devoid of infinite knowledges, it does not follow that he is characterized by infinite opposites, because the subsistence of an opposite is only in place of what the locus was capable of. Al-Amidi said: This is even worse than the answer of al-Ustadh. The author said: (And this answer is only valid) if the existence of what is infinite as a succession were impossible, just as its existence (together is impossible), but that has not been established. And it was answered: that the necessary then is the characterization of the servant with attributes from the heart[?] that is impossible.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof of those who permit a body to be devoid of accidents, and multiple responses. The proof: God can create in a human knowledge of any known thing. The known things are infinite (God, possibles, impossibles), so knowledges would be infinite. But human knowledge is finite. If a body cannot be devoid of an accident and its opposite, then for every absent knowledge, its opposite must be present, leading to infinite attributes in the human—which is impossible. The same applies to powers (qudra) and volitions (irada). The first response distinguishes: what is absent is the 'relation of knowledge' (ta'alliq al-'ilm), which is not an accident but an i'tibari (relational) thing. So no infinite opposites follow. Also, one knowledge can relate to many known things, so infinite knowledges are not required. Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq responds based on his principle of opposition of knowledges: the opposite of all the absent infinite knowledges is the single (or multiple) knowledge that is present, so no infinite opposites. This commits him to the impossibility of two knowledges in one locus, which he accepts. Ibn Furak responds: humans only accept finite knowledges; they are not capable of infinite knowledges, so the absence of infinite knowledges does not imply infinite opposites because the opposite only arises where the locus is capable. Al-Amidi criticizes this as worse. The author (al-Iji?) comments that Ibn Furak's response would only work if the impossibility of infinite successive existences were established, which it is not. A final answer is given: the necessary consequence would be that the servant is characterized by impossible attributes, but that fails due to the impossibility of infinite accidents.

Key Terms
معلوم (known thing)
علم (knowledge)
متناه (finite)
غير متناه (infinite)
ضد (opposite)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof claims that humans actually have infinite attributes.
Correction: The proof is a conditional argument: if the opponent's principle (no empty of accident and opposite) holds, then the human would have infinite attributes, which is impossible. It does not assert that humans actually have infinite attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the proof concludes that infinite opposite attributes would subsist in the human?
A) Humans have infinite knowledge
B) For each absent knowledge, its opposite must be present due to the impossibility of being devoid of an accident and its opposite
C) God creates opposite attributes directly
D) The human is capable of infinite opposites

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that if a body cannot be empty of accidents and opposites, then infinite known things would force infinite opposite attributes in a finite human, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that a finite human cannot bear infinite attributes. Where in your life have you claimed to have complete knowledge or control, and what opposite attribute—like arrogance or self-sufficiency—might be hiding beneath that claim? How will you humble yourself before God today?
?
If the absence of knowledge forces a corresponding opposite into your soul, what are the gaps in your knowledge of Allah that you have neglected, and what opposite attitude (like indifference or doubt) have you allowed to settle in their place? What concrete step will you take to replace that opposite with knowledge?

4 min
First Response: Knowledge Relation is Relational (I'tibari)
First answer: the absent relation is not a real accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first response to the second proof: what is absent is the relation of knowledge (ta'alluq), which is not a real accident but a relational (i'tibari) entity, so no infinite opposites follow.

Core Idea
Knowledge relation is not a real accident; it is a relational concept.

After presenting the second proof, the author provides the first response. The response distinguishes between knowledge as an existent attribute and the relation of knowledge to its object. The objector claims that what is absent from the human is the relation of knowledge to infinite known things, and this relation is not an accident but a relational (i'tibari) entity. Therefore, the absence does not necessitate the presence of an opposite attribute. Moreover, one knowledge can relate to multiple known things, so infinite knowledges are not required.

Guided Path
1
Read the response carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the distinction between 't'alluq al-'ilm' (relation of knowledge) and 'ilm' (knowledge as a property).
The key phrase: 'ليس بعرض بل هو أمر اعتباري'.
2
Understand the two parts of the response
First, the objector denies that the absent thing is a real accident. Second, they assert that one knowledge can relate to many known things, eliminating the need for infinite separate knowledges.
3
See how this refutes the proof
Since the opposite of a relational entity is not a real accident, no infinite opposite accidents are generated. The proof's reductio collapses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثاني : ما من معلوم إلا ويمكن أن يخلق الله تعالى في العبد علماً .به والمعلومات) أي المفهومات التي يمكن أن يتعلق العلم بها ( في نفسها غير متناهية لشمولها الواجب والممكنات والممتنعات فكذا العلوم المتعلقة بها غير متناهية ) والحاصل من تلك العلوم ( للعبد متناه ) لاستحالة وجود ما لا يتناهى ) فإن انتفى ) والظاهر أن يقال : فقد انتفى عنه علوم غير متناهية فكان يجب ) على تقدير امتناع الخلو عن العرض وضده ( أن يقوم به بإزاء كل علم منتف عنه ضد له (فيلزم حينئذ (قيام صفات غير متناهية ) بالعبد، (وكذا الحال ( في المقدورات (ونحوها كالمرادات . ( وأنه محال) لما عرفت ) والجواب أن المنتفي عن العبد هو ( تعلق العلم بما لا يتناهى من المعلومات ( وأنه ( أي ذلك التعلق ( ليس بعرض بل هو أمر اعتباري ( وهذا ) الإلزام الذي ذكرتموه ( إنما يلزم من يحوج كل معلوم إلى علم على حده ويجعله مع أمراً موجوداً لا نفس التعلق الاعتباري ) ونحن لا نقول به بل يجوز أن يتعلق علم واحد بمعلومات متعددة أو نجعله نفس التعلق لا صفة موجودة . ( وأجاب الأستاذ أبو إسحاق بناء على أصله من تضاد العلوم (المتعددة وإن كانت مختلفة لا متماثلة أن ) أي بأن (ضد العلوم (المنتفية التي لا تتناهى ) هو العلم الحاصل سواء كان متعدداً أو واحداً فلا محذور . ( وألزم ) الأستاذ على أصله ( امتناع اجتماع علمين) مطلقاً محل واحد لكونهما متضادين عنده فالتزمه وزعم أن لكل علم محلا غير ما للآخر فلا يجتمع علمان في محل واحد أصلاً ( وأجاب ابن فورك) فقال: المعلومات وإن كانت غير متناهية فالإنسان لا يقبل منها إلا علوما متناهية لامتناع وجود ما لا يتناهى مطلقاً وإذا لم يقبل ما لا يتناهى من العلوم لم يلزم على تقدير خلوه من العلوم التي لا تتناهي أن يتصف بأضداد غير متناهية لأن قيام الضد إنما يكون بدل ما كان المحل قابلاً له قال الآمدي وهذا أشد من جواب الأستاذ . قال المصنف : ( وإنما يصح ) هذا الجواب ( لو امتنع وجود ما لا يتناهى بدلاً كما وجوده (معاً لكنه لم يثبت، وأجيب عنه : بأن اللازم حينئذ اتصاف العبد بصفات من القلب[؟] يمتنع
The second aspect: There is no known thing except that God (exalted) could create in the servant knowledge of it. And the known things) i.e., the concepts that knowledge can relate to (are in themselves infinite due to their encompassing the necessary, the possible, and the impossible. So likewise the knowledges related to them are infinite. And the sum of those knowledges (for the servant is finite) due to the impossibility of existence of what is infinite. So if it is absent) (the apparent thing to say: he is devoid of infinite knowledges. So it would be necessary, (on the assumption of the impossibility of being devoid of an accident and its opposite) that in exchange for every knowledge that is absent, its opposite should subsist in him. (Then it would follow) (the subsistence of infinite attributes) in the servant. (And likewise the case) (in the things that are within his power, and similar things like volitions). (And that is impossible) as you know. (And the answer: What is absent from the servant is) (the relation of knowledge to infinite known things (and that)) (i.e., that relation is not an accident but an i'tibari (relational) thing. (And this) objection that you have mentioned (only applies to one who necessitates for every known thing a separate knowledge and makes it an existent attribute, not the mere i'tibari relation). And we do not say that; rather, it is permissible for one knowledge to relate to multiple known things, or we make it the very relation, not an existent attribute. (And al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq answered based on his principle of the opposition of knowledges, (that) (the opposite of the absent knowledges that are infinite) is the knowledge that is obtained, whether it is multiple or one, so there is no problem. (And al-Ustadh was compelled by his principle) (to assert the impossibility of two knowledges coming together) absolutely in one locus, because they are opposites according to him, so he affirmed it and claimed that every knowledge has a locus other than that of the other, so two knowledges never come together in one locus at all. (And Ibn Furak answered) and said: Even if the known things are infinite, man only accepts from them finite knowledges due to the impossibility of the existence of what is infinite absolutely. And if he does not accept infinite knowledges, then on the assumption of his being devoid of infinite knowledges, it does not follow that he is characterized by infinite opposites, because the subsistence of an opposite is only in place of what the locus was capable of. Al-Amidi said: This is even worse than the answer of al-Ustadh. The author said: (And this answer is only valid) if the existence of what is infinite as a succession were impossible, just as its existence (together is impossible), but that has not been established. And it was answered: that the necessary then is the characterization of the servant with attributes from the heart[?] that is impossible.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof of those who permit a body to be devoid of accidents, and multiple responses. The proof: God can create in a human knowledge of any known thing. The known things are infinite (God, possibles, impossibles), so knowledges would be infinite. But human knowledge is finite. If a body cannot be devoid of an accident and its opposite, then for every absent knowledge, its opposite must be present, leading to infinite attributes in the human—which is impossible. The same applies to powers (qudra) and volitions (irada). The first response distinguishes: what is absent is the 'relation of knowledge' (ta'alliq al-'ilm), which is not an accident but an i'tibari (relational) thing. So no infinite opposites follow. Also, one knowledge can relate to many known things, so infinite knowledges are not required. Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq responds based on his principle of opposition of knowledges: the opposite of all the absent infinite knowledges is the single (or multiple) knowledge that is present, so no infinite opposites. This commits him to the impossibility of two knowledges in one locus, which he accepts. Ibn Furak responds: humans only accept finite knowledges; they are not capable of infinite knowledges, so the absence of infinite knowledges does not imply infinite opposites because the opposite only arises where the locus is capable. Al-Amidi criticizes this as worse. The author (al-Iji?) comments that Ibn Furak's response would only work if the impossibility of infinite successive existences were established, which it is not. A final answer is given: the necessary consequence would be that the servant is characterized by impossible attributes, but that fails due to the impossibility of infinite accidents.

Key Terms
تعلق (relation)
علم (knowledge)
اعتباري (relational)
عرض (accident)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The absence of knowledge relation is the absence of a real accident.
Correction: The response distinguishes that the relation is not a real accident but a relational concept; thus, its absence does not necessitate an opposite real accident.

Checkpoint
How does the first response refute the necessity of infinite opposite attributes?
A) It claims that absent relation is a real accident, so opposites are needed
B) It argues that the relation is i'tibari, so its absence does not require a real opposite
C) It says that knowledge cannot relate to multiple things
D) It accepts the proof but says it is impossible

Key Takeaway
The first response denies that the absence of knowledge relation is an absence of a real accident, and allows one knowledge to relate to many objects, dissolving the proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen that God's knowledge relation to every detail of your life is not a real accident that can be absent—it is a relational concept that encompasses all at once. Does your daily prayer reflect this certainty, or do you act as if God overlooks some of your struggles? What one habit can you change today to live with this awareness?
?
If the relation of knowledge is not a real attribute, then the absence of feeling knowledge in a moment of doubt does not mean God is unaware of you. How does this distinction challenge your tendency to judge His presence by your emotional state? What step can you take to trust His knowledge even when you feel distant?
?
The argument shows that one knowledge relates to infinite things. Your life, with all its hidden thoughts and actions, is fully present before God at once. Are you comfortable with this, or does it make you uneasy? What secret are you harboring that you need to repent from, knowing that God's relation to it is immediate and complete?

4 min
Second Response: Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq's Opposition Principle
Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq's response: present knowledge is opposite to all absent ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq's response based on the mutual opposition of knowledges and its consequence that two knowledges cannot coexist in one locus.

Core Idea
All absent knowledges are opposite to the single present knowledge, so no infinite opposites.

Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq offers a different response based on his principle that knowledges are mutually opposed (mutadādda), even if they are different in kind, not just identical. According to him, the opposite of all the infinite absent knowledges is the knowledge that is actually present in the servant, whether that present knowledge is one or multiple. Therefore, no infinite opposite attributes are required—only the present knowledge(s) serve as the opposite for all absent ones. However, this principle commits him to the impossibility of two knowledges coexisting in the same locus, which he accepts.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Ustadh's answer
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice his assumption: all knowledges are opposites, even if different.
The key phrase: 'ضد العلوم المنتفية هو العلم الحاصل'.
2
Understand the implication
Since the present knowledge is the opposite of all absent knowledges, no infinite opposites are needed. But this leads to a corollary: two knowledges cannot coexist.
3
Evaluate the consequence
Al-Ustadh explicitly accepts that two knowledges cannot be in one locus; each knowledge has its own locus. Consider whether this is plausible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ustadh's response allows multiple knowledges in the same locus.
Correction: His principle of opposition forces him to deny the coexistence of two knowledges in one locus.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of al-Ustadh's principle of the opposition of knowledges?
A) Two knowledges can coexist in one locus
B) Two knowledges cannot coexist in one locus
C) Knowledge is not an accident
D) There is only one knowledge

Key Takeaway
Al-Ustadh avoids infinite opposites by claiming present knowledge is opposite to all absent ones, but this forces him to deny that two knowledges can coexist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold two seemingly contradictory religious beliefs (e.g., divine predestination and human free will), do you treat them as opposites that cannot be together in your mind, or do you seek a deeper unity? How does al-Ustadh's radical move of assigning each knowledge to a different locus challenge you to honestly confront your own intellectual inconsistencies?
?
If you were forced to concede that two of your deeply held convictions cannot coexist in the same 'place' of your heart, which one would you sacrifice? Are you willing to let go of a cherished belief for the sake of consistency, or would you rather compartmentalize—and what does that say about your pursuit of truth?

5 min
Third Response: Ibn Furak and Its Critique
Ibn Furak's finite capacity response and its critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Furak's response that humans only accept finite knowledges, so infinite opposites do not follow, and the subsequent critiques by al-Amidi and the author.

Core Idea
Humans are incapable of infinite knowledges, so no infinite opposites can arise.

Ibn Furak offers a third response: although known things are infinite, the human mind can only accept a finite number of knowledges. Since the human is incapable of infinite knowledges, the absence of infinite knowledges does not imply the presence of infinite opposites—because an opposite only arises when the locus is capable of receiving the original. Al-Amidi criticizes this as worse than al-Ustadh's answer. The author (likely al-Iji) comments that Ibn Furak's response would only work if the impossibility of infinite successive existences were established, which it is not. A final argument is mentioned but its text is unclear.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Furak's response
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice his key claim: 'la yaqbal minha illa 'uluman mutanahiya'.
2
Understand al-Amidi's criticism
Al-Amidi says this answer is even worse than al-Ustadh's. Why? Because it seems to ignore the possibility that the human could have been capable of more.
3
Examine the author's comment
The author points out that Ibn Furak's response would be valid only if the impossibility of infinite successive existences were proven, but that is not established. Then a final argument is mentioned, though the text is corrupt.
The final line 'من القلب' is uncertain.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

( وأنه محال) لما عرفت ) والجواب أن المنتفي عن العبد هو ( تعلق العلم بما لا يتناهى من المعلومات ( وأنه ( أي ذلك التعلق ( ليس بعرض بل هو أمر اعتباري ( وهذا ) الإلزام الذي ذكرتموه ( إنما يلزم من يحوج كل معلوم إلى علم على حده ويجعله مع أمراً موجوداً لا نفس التعلق الاعتباري ) ونحن لا نقول به بل يجوز أن يتعلق علم واحد بمعلومات متعددة أو نجعله نفس التعلق لا صفة موجودة . ( وأجاب الأستاذ أبو إسحاق بناء على أصله من تضاد العلوم (المتعددة وإن كانت مختلفة لا متماثلة أن ) أي بأن (ضد العلوم (المنتفية التي لا تتناهى ) هو العلم الحاصل سواء كان متعدداً أو واحداً فلا محذور . ( وألزم ) الأستاذ على أصله ( امتناع اجتماع علمين) مطلقاً محل واحد لكونهما متضادين عنده فالتزمه وزعم أن لكل علم محلا غير ما للآخر فلا يجتمع علمان في محل واحد أصلاً ( وأجاب ابن فورك) فقال: المعلومات وإن كانت غير متناهية فالإنسان لا يقبل منها إلا علوما متناهية لامتناع وجود ما لا يتناهى مطلقاً وإذا لم يقبل ما لا يتناهى من العلوم لم يلزم على تقدير خلوه من العلوم التي لا تتناهي أن يتصف بأضداد غير متناهية لأن قيام الضد إنما يكون بدل ما كان المحل قابلاً له قال الآمدي وهذا أشد من جواب الأستاذ . قال المصنف : ( وإنما يصح ) هذا الجواب ( لو امتنع وجود ما لا يتناهى بدلاً كما وجوده (معاً لكنه لم يثبت، وأجيب عنه : بأن اللازم حينئذ اتصاف العبد بصفات من القلب[؟] يمتنع
The second aspect: There is no known thing except that God (exalted) could create in the servant knowledge of it. And the known things) i.e., the concepts that knowledge can relate to (are in themselves infinite due to their encompassing the necessary, the possible, and the impossible. So likewise the knowledges related to them are infinite. And the sum of those knowledges (for the servant is finite) due to the impossibility of existence of what is infinite. So if it is absent) (the apparent thing to say: he is devoid of infinite knowledges. So it would be necessary, (on the assumption of the impossibility of being devoid of an accident and its opposite) that in exchange for every knowledge that is absent, its opposite should subsist in him. (Then it would follow) (the subsistence of infinite attributes) in the servant. (And likewise the case) (in the things that are within his power, and similar things like volitions). (And that is impossible) as you know. (And the answer: What is absent from the servant is) (the relation of knowledge to infinite known things (and that)) (i.e., that relation is not an accident but an i'tibari (relational) thing. (And this) objection that you have mentioned (only applies to one who necessitates for every known thing a separate knowledge and makes it an existent attribute, not the mere i'tibari relation). And we do not say that; rather, it is permissible for one knowledge to relate to multiple known things, or we make it the very relation, not an existent attribute. (And al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq answered based on his principle of the opposition of knowledges, (that) (the opposite of the absent knowledges that are infinite) is the knowledge that is obtained, whether it is multiple or one, so there is no problem. (And al-Ustadh was compelled by his principle) (to assert the impossibility of two knowledges coming together) absolutely in one locus, because they are opposites according to him, so he affirmed it and claimed that every knowledge has a locus other than that of the other, so two knowledges never come together in one locus at all. (And Ibn Furak answered) and said: Even if the known things are infinite, man only accepts from them finite knowledges due to the impossibility of the existence of what is infinite absolutely. And if he does not accept infinite knowledges, then on the assumption of his being devoid of infinite knowledges, it does not follow that he is characterized by infinite opposites, because the subsistence of an opposite is only in place of what the locus was capable of. Al-Amidi said: This is even worse than the answer of al-Ustadh. The author said: (And this answer is only valid) if the existence of what is infinite as a succession were impossible, just as its existence (together is impossible), but that has not been established. And it was answered: that the necessary then is the characterization of the servant with attributes from the heart[?] that is impossible.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof of those who permit a body to be devoid of accidents, and multiple responses. The proof: God can create in a human knowledge of any known thing. The known things are infinite (God, possibles, impossibles), so knowledges would be infinite. But human knowledge is finite. If a body cannot be devoid of an accident and its opposite, then for every absent knowledge, its opposite must be present, leading to infinite attributes in the human—which is impossible. The same applies to powers (qudra) and volitions (irada). The first response distinguishes: what is absent is the 'relation of knowledge' (ta'alliq al-'ilm), which is not an accident but an i'tibari (relational) thing. So no infinite opposites follow. Also, one knowledge can relate to many known things, so infinite knowledges are not required. Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq responds based on his principle of opposition of knowledges: the opposite of all the absent infinite knowledges is the single (or multiple) knowledge that is present, so no infinite opposites. This commits him to the impossibility of two knowledges in one locus, which he accepts. Ibn Furak responds: humans only accept finite knowledges; they are not capable of infinite knowledges, so the absence of infinite knowledges does not imply infinite opposites because the opposite only arises where the locus is capable. Al-Amidi criticizes this as worse. The author (al-Iji?) comments that Ibn Furak's response would only work if the impossibility of infinite successive existences were established, which it is not. A final answer is given: the necessary consequence would be that the servant is characterized by impossible attributes, but that fails due to the impossibility of infinite accidents.

Key Terms
متناه (finite)
غير متناه (infinite)
قابل (capable)
بدل (succession)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Furak's response successfully refutes the proof without remainder.
Correction: The author and al-Amidi both find problems: al-Amidi says it's worse, and the author points out an unproven assumption about infinite succession.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Amidi consider Ibn Furak's answer worse than al-Ustadh's?
A) Because it denies that humans can have any knowledge
B) Because it seems to assume that humans cannot be made capable of infinite knowledge, which is not obviously true
C) Because it accepts infinite opposites
D) Because it relies on a corrupt text

Key Takeaway
Ibn Furak's finite capacity response is criticized for assuming without proof that infinite successive existence is impossible, and al-Amidi considers it even weaker than al-Ustadh's answer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that your knowledge is finite, but do you live as though you can fully comprehend God's ways? In what areas of your faith have you relied on your own reasoning rather than revelation? Will you today admit your intellectual limits and submit to what God has taught?
?
The critique suggests that infinite successive existence is not proven impossible. How does the reality of an eternal afterlife—an infinite future—change what you do today? Are you living as if every moment echoes into eternity? Make one concrete change in your daily routine to reflect that truth.
?
Ibn Furak's response was considered weak for lacking proof. Have you ever held a theological opinion without solid evidence from revelation or sound reason? What would it cost you to examine your beliefs and discard what is not firmly grounded? Commit to one step toward seeking certain knowledge.

The Second Proof and the Relational Answer
Al-Iji presents the second proof and the first Ash'ari response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof against the Ash'ari position that a body must have an accident or its opposite, and the school's initial response that what is negated is only a relational attachment, not an existent accident.

Core Idea
The impossible infinite is merely the negation of a relation, not an accident requiring an opposite.

This lesson introduces the second proof and the primary Ash'ari reply. The proof argues that since there are infinite possible objects of knowledge, a human would need infinite opposite attributes if freedom from accident and opposite were impossible. The reply reinterprets what is negated as a mere relation, thereby avoiding the need for infinite opposites.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
The proof states that knowledge can be created for the servant regarding any known thing, and known things are infinite. Hence the sciences related to them would be infinite, but the servant's finite knowledge means infinite sciences are negated. If freedom from accident and opposite is impossible, each negated science would require an opposite in the servant, leading to infinite attributes — which is impossible.
Notice the key assumption: the impossibility of being free from an accident and its opposite.
2
Identify the first response
The response argues that what is negated is the 'attachment of knowledge' (talluq), which is not an accident but a relational consideration (amr i'tibari). Therefore, no opposite attribute is needed because the negation of a relation does not demand an opposite accident.
The response distinguishes between existent attributes and relational considerations.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this response defends the original principle (that a body cannot be free from accident and opposite) by denying that the negation of infinite sciences entails infinite opposites. The opponent's objection is answered without abandoning the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : ما من معلوم إلا ويمكن أن يخلق الله تعالى في العبد علماً به والمعلومات أي المفهومات التي يمكن أن يتعلق العلم بها في نفسها غير متناهية لشمولها الواجب والممكنات والممتنعات فكذا العلوم المتعلقة بها غير متناهية والحاصل من تلك العلوم للعبد متناه لاستحالة وجود ما لا يتناهى فإن انتفى والظاهر أن يقال : فقد انتفى عنه علوم غير متناهية فكان يجب على تقدير امتناع الخلو عن العرض وضده أن يقوم به بإزاء كل علم منتف عنه ضد له فيلزم حينئذ قيام صفات غير متناهية بالعبد، وكذا الحال في المقدورات ونحوها كالمرادات . وأنه محال لما عرفت . والجواب أن المنتفي عن العبد هو تعلق العلم بما لا يتناهى من المعلومات وأنه أي ذلك التعلق ليس بعرض بل هو أمر اعتباري وهذا الإلزام الذي ذكرتموه إنما يلزم من يحوج كل معلوم إلى علم على حده ويجعله مع أمراً موجوداً لا نفس التعلق الاعتباري ونحن لا نقول به بل يجوز أن يتعلق علم واحد بمعلومات متعددة أو نجعله نفس التعلق لا صفة موجودة . وأجاب الأستاذ أبو إسحاق بناء على أصله من تضاد العلوم المتعددة وإن كانت مختلفة لا متماثلة أن ضد العلوم المنتفية التي لا تتناهى هو العلم الحاصل سواء كان متعدداً أو واحداً فلا محذور . وألزم الأستاذ على أصله امتناع اجتماع علمين مطلقاً محل واحد لكونهما متضادين عنده فالتزمه وزعم أن لكل علم محلا غير ما للآخر فلا يجتمع علمان في محل واحد أصلاً . وأجاب ابن فورك فقال: المعلومات وإن كانت غير متناهية فالإنسان لا يقبل منها إلا علوما متناهية لامتناع وجود ما لا يتناهى مطلقاً وإذا لم يقبل ما لا يتناهى من العلوم لم يلزم على تقدير خلوه من العلوم التي لا تتناهي أن يتصف بأضداد غير متناهية لأن قيام الضد إنما يكون بدل ما كان المحل قابلاً له قال الآمدي وهذا أشد من جواب الأستاذ . قال المصنف : وإنما يصح هذا الجواب لو امتنع وجود ما لا يتناهى بدلاً كما وجوده معاً لكنه لم يثبت، وأجيب عنه : بأن اللازم حينئذ اتصاف العبد بصفات غير متناهية على سبيل البدل وليس بمستحيل لأن الحاصل للعبد في كل وقت مع ما قبله من الأوقات متناه قطعاً . وأجاب القاضي الباقلاني بأنه قد يكون انتفاء ما انتفى عنه من العلوم التي لا تتناهى بضد عام هو صفة واحدة مضادة لجميع تلك العلوم المنتفية ولا استحالة في مثل ذلك كالموت والنوم فإنهما ضدان لجميع العلوم على الإطلاق وإذا جاز ذلك جاز أيضاً أن تضاد صفة واحدة ما عدا العلوم الحاصلة.
The second proof: There is no known thing except that Allah Most High can create in the servant knowledge of it. And the known things — that is, the concepts to which knowledge can attach — are in themselves infinite because they include the Necessary, the possibles, and the impossibles. So the sciences related to them are likewise infinite. But the amount of those sciences obtained by the servant is finite, because it is impossible for something infinite to exist. If then it is negated — and it would seem to be said: there are negated from him infinite sciences — then, on the assumption that freedom from an accident and its opposite is impossible, there must arise in him, corresponding to each negated science, its opposite. Hence there would follow the existence of infinite attributes in the servant. And the same holds for the objects of power and the like, such as objects of will. And that is impossible, as you have known. The answer: What is negated from the servant is the attachment of knowledge to infinite known things, and that attachment is not an accident but a relational consideration. This implication that you mention only follows for one who requires for every known thing a separate knowledge and makes it an existent attribute, not the mere relational consideration. But we do not say that; rather, we allow that a single knowledge may attach to multiple known things, or we make it the mere relational attachment and not an existent attribute. And the master Abu Ishaq answered on the basis of his principle of the mutual opposition of multiple sciences, even if they are different and not identical: that the opposite of the negated sciences, which are infinite, is the acquired science, whether it is multiple or single, so there is no absurdity. And he (the master) was compelled by his principle to hold that two sciences cannot come together in a single subject at all, since they are opposites according to him; and he accepted that and claimed that each science has a subject other than the other, so no two sciences ever meet in one subject. And Ibn Furak answered, saying: Even if the known things are infinite, a human cannot accept from them but finite sciences, because it is impossible for something infinite to exist absolutely. And if he does not accept infinite sciences, then on the supposition of his being free from the infinite sciences, it does not necessitate that he be characterized by infinite opposites, because the existence of an opposite is only as a substitute for what the subject was capable of. Al-Amidi said: This is more severe than the answer of the master. The author said: This answer would be valid only if the existence of the infinite as a substitute were impossible, just as its existence simultaneously is impossible, but that has not been established. And it was answered: In that case the necessary consequence would be the servant's being characterized by infinite attributes on a substitute basis, and that is not impossible because what is obtained by the servant at every time together with what preceded it from times is finite, definitely. And the Qadi al-Baqillani answered: It is possible that the negation of what is negated from him of the infinite sciences be by a universal opposite — that is, a single attribute opposite to all those negated sciences — and there is no impossibility in that, like death and sleep, for they are opposites to all sciences absolutely. And if that is allowed, it is also allowed that a single attribute be opposite to all sciences except those that are acquired.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (الوجه الثاني) of the sixth topic, arguing that if a body cannot be devoid of an accident and its opposite, then the human subject would have to have infinite attributes. The proof is based on the infinite range of objects of knowledge: since there are infinite possible known things, the sciences (acts of knowledge) corresponding to them would also be infinite. But the human can only have finite knowledge, so infinite sciences are negated from him. On the assumption that he cannot be free from both an accident and its opposite, each negated science would require a corresponding opposite attribute existing in him, leading to an infinite number of attributes, which is impossible. The author then presents multiple responses from Ash'ari scholars. The first response (attributed to the author or the school) is that what is negated is merely the relational attachment of knowledge, not an existent accident, so no opposite is needed. The second is from Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini, who relies on the mutual opposition of sciences: the opposite of all those negated sciences is simply the actual knowledge that the person has, whether one or many. The third is from Ibn Furak, who argues that the human can only accept a finite number of sciences, so no infinite opposites arise. The author criticizes this, noting that the impossibility of infinite existence as a substitute has not been proven. A subsequent answer addresses that: even if infinite attributes replaced each other over time, each moment is finite. Finally, al-Qadi al-Baqillani suggests a universal opposite: a single attribute (like death or sleep) that opposes all sciences, so only one opposite is needed. These responses show the flexibility of Ash'ari theology in defending its principles against objections.

Key Terms
العلم
التعلق الاعتباري
العرض
الضد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second proof shows that the Ash'ari principle is false because it leads to infinite attributes.
Correction: The response clarifies that the supposed infinite attributes are not required because what is negated is a relation, not an existent accident.

Checkpoint
According to the first response, what is negated from the servant is:
A) An infinite number of existent sciences.
B) The attachment of knowledge, which is a relational consideration.
C) The opposite of each negated science.
D) The ability to know infinite objects.

Key Takeaway
The first response defends the Ash'ari principle by arguing that the negation of infinite knowledge attachments does not entail infinite opposite attributes, because a relational consideration does not require an opposite accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that infinite knowledges are negated from you, but that negation does not bring a flood of opposing attributes. How does this reshape your view of your own ignorance before Allah? Does it make you more aware of His infinite knowledge, or does it tempt you to be complacent about what you do not know? What will you do today to seek one piece of knowledge that brings you closer to Him?
?
The Ash'ari response says the attachment of knowledge is a relational consideration, not a separate accident. Think about your own beliefs: do you treat your knowledge as a possession you own, or as a relation to the truth that can grow and change without threatening your identity? What would it look like to live more lightly, as someone whose knowledge is a relation to Allah's truth rather than a fixed attribute?
?
If your limited knowledge does not force you to be filled with its opposites, then what does it mean to be a finite creature before the Infinite? Does this silence the fear that your imperfections contradict your faith? Or does it challenge you to embrace your limits as a sign of your dependence on Allah? What one step will you take today to live out that dependence?
